Title: Building and maintaining a System of Intracellular Compartments

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.12930v1

Published Time: Wed, 15 Apr 2026 01:37:24 GMT

Number of Pages: 55

Markdown Content:
Building and maintaining a System of Intracellular Compartments 

Amit Kumar 1,2 and Madan Rao 2∗

> 1

Raman Research Institute, C.V. Raman Avenue, Bengaluru 560080, India 

> 2

Centre for Living Machines, National Centre for Biological Sciences (TIFR), GKVK Campus, Bellary Road, Bengaluru 560065, India 

Organelle patterning and its heritability remain central mysteries in cell biology, highlighting the fundamental tension between genetic inheritance and self-assembly. Here, we explore the nonequilib-rium assembly and size control of the Golgi complex and endosomes, amid a continuous flux of mem-brane traffic, within a stochastic framework of mechanochemical fusion-fission cycles that violate detailed balance. Using a dynamical systems approach, we identify distinct, robust regimes, ranging from fixed points to limit cycles with definite phase relations. We identify these dynamical regimes with diverse phenotypes, from stable cisternae to periodic, cell-cycle-dependent dissolution/reassem-bly to cisternal progression. We analyse its dynamic response to systematic perturbations or driving protocols and make definite predictions that may be tested experimentally. Our analysis reveals that the two competing models of Golgi organization—vesicular transport and cisternal progression—are, in fact, two phases of the same underlying nonequilibrium process. Finally, our framework offers a strategy for controlling cisternal chemical identity and number and by modulating the interplay between glycosylation enzymes and membrane fission-fusion dynamics. 

I. INTRODUCTION 

Understanding how subcellular structures of definite size, shape and chemical identity are assembled and maintained under nonequilibrium conditions remains a fundamental challenge in cell biology [1–3]. The assembly and homeostatic control of organelles, such as the Golgi complex and Endosomes, are of particular interest [4]; these membrane-bound compartments must reliably maintain their identity despite a continuous vectorial flux of vesicles driven by active trans-port, fusion and fission. Despite detailed knowledge of the molecular players involved in their membrane remodeling, the key physical principles underlying organelle assembly in such open systems remain unclear [5]. The objective of this paper is to arrive at a quantitative description that clearly lays out the organising principles governing the assembly of the system of Golgi cisternae and make testable predictions. In doing so, we arrive at some surprising insights into intracellular organisation. Over the years, many descriptive models of Golgi organisation have been proposed, such as vesicular transport [6] and cisternal progression [7–9], often portrayed as being contesting [10, 11]. Interpretations of these models using computational and physics-based approaches to qualitatively and quantitatively confront with experiments, by and large do not explore the space of steady states as a function of fusion-fission parameters or their dynamical stability, nor do they study feedback control mechanisms that maintain the nonequilibrium steady state; see however [10, 12, 13]. In our view, the distinctive feature of the Golgi nonequilibrium assembly is that its dynamical steady state is a system of cisternae in the presence of a finite vesicular flux [14], that is robust to cellular noise. Our analysis draws inspiration from recent discussions on the nonequilibrium assembly and size control of the diversity of filamentous organelles in eukaryotic cells, such as flagella, cilia, filopodia, which are built from the assembly-disassembly of microtubule or actin based structures [15–19]. The takeaway from these studies is that homeostasis of structures at a fixed size can be achieved only with size-dependent assembly/disassembly rates [15]. In this paper, we study the stochastic time evolution of the sizes of the Golgi cisternae subject to nonequilibrium fusion and fission modelled as discrete Markov cycles. By integrating over the cycle time of the “fast” fusion-fission processes, we derive dynamical mean field equations for the cisternal sizes. The active mechanochemistry of the fusion-fission cycles naturally gives rise to a size-dependent negative feedback control via fusion-fission kernels. This embedded feed-forward control successfully maintains stable cisternae in the presence of a net vectorial flux of vesicles. The dynamical system for multiple cisternae shows additional nonequilibrium steady states, including limit cycles with definite phase relations between cisternae. We identify such nonequilibrium steady states with distinct phenotypic outcomes such as vesicle transport, cisternal progression, or the periodic dissolution and reformation of cisternae across the cell cycle. We analyse its dynamic response to systematic perturbations or driving protocols and make definite predictions that may be tested experimentally. Our work applies to cisternal dynamics and their organisation in a variety of cellular contexts, both in health and in disease. Finally, we study the robustness of this system of cisternae to extrinsic (influx)  

> ∗rao.madan@gmail.com
> arXiv:2604.12930v1 [cond-mat.soft] 14 Apr 2026

2and intrinsic (chemical) noise, using analytic approaches and Gillespie simulations. Taken as a whole, our study argues that the Vesicular Transport and Cisternal Progression models and their variants are different phenotypic outcomes of the same underlying physical description that must occupy different locations in a nonequilibrium phase diagram. For mathematical derivations and analyses of the geometry of flows of dynamical systems, the reader may consult S1-S7 of the Supplementary Information (SI). For convenience, we display the numerical values of parameters used in the study in Table S1 and a compact Glossary 1 of dynamical systems nomenclature.  

> II. STOCHASTIC DESCRIPTION OF THE NONEQUILIBRIUM ASSEMBLY OF CISTERNAE

The buildup and maintenance of Golgi cisternae is a consequence of three major nonequilibrium processes, namely, the continual anterograde-retrograde flux of (small, 50 nm diameter, spherical and/or larger tubular) transport vesicles injected from the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) towards the plasma membrane (PM) and the active processes of fusion and fission of these vesicles to and from cisternae (Fig. 1(a)) mediated by specific GTP binding enzymes and specialised proteins, such as Rab proteins, tethering proteins, v-SNARE and t-SNARE for fusion [20, 21] and Arf and COP proteins for fission [22–24]. We study the kinetics of nonequilibrium assembly at time scales longer than enzymatic cycle time scales [25, 26] and ignore details of the various molecules involved in fusion and fission – we will collectively refer to these enzymes and their associated proteins as fusogens and fisogens , respectively. Each fusion (fission) event adds (removes) membrane area and lumenal volume to (from) the cisternae, referred to as addition (removal) of cisternal “mass” or “size” [10, 12]. We first describe the kinetics of nonequilibrium assembly of a single Golgi cisterna of size 𝑀 (in units of typical transport vesicle size) in terms of a stochastic master equation that incorporates the physics of fusion and fission via discrete Markov cycles (for the master equation for 𝑛 -cisternae, see S1.1 and S3). These cycles, defined by internal states and inter-state transitions, Fig. 1(b,c), are as follows: Upon activation from an external state (labelled |0⟩), the local cisternal membrane configuration associated with fusogens ( + species) or fisogens ( − species) could transition into an ordered sequence of internal states that are indexed by 𝑖 ∈ { 1, 2, 3, . . . , ℵ± }, where ℵ± is the number of internal states, before winding back to the external state |0⟩, terminating this cycle and priming for the next. We make the following assumptions, supported empirically – (i) Vesicles of unit size are injected from the ER at a specified rate, constituting a net vectorial current from the ER (left) to the PM (right) [5, 27–30] (Fig. 1(a)). (ii) Vesicles injected from the ER fuse at a preassigned nucleation site with a given rate to initiate a growing cisterna [31]. (iii) Initiation of the Fusion and Fission cycles are independent stochastic events, catalysed by distinct sets of enzymes. (iv) There is a finite cisternal pool of fusogens and fisogens, set by expression levels of the cell.(v) Internal state transitions in the fusion-fission cycle are fast (milliseconds-seconds [25, 26]) compared to the time scale over which cisternal size is updated (seconds-minutes [9]). (vi) Fusion and Fission are fast and so we assume that the fusion or fission events are discrete and non-overlapping. In S1.1, we consider the case of multiple fusion-fission events in a time interval Δ𝑡 over which the cisternal size is updated. With these assumptions, we write down a master equation describing the time evolution of the probability distribution 

𝑃 (𝑀, 𝑖, 𝑘, 𝑡 | 𝑁 v, 𝑁 +, 𝑁 − ) of a cisterna of size 𝑀 at specific internal states 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , ℵ+ } and 𝑘 ∈ { 1, . . . , ℵ− }, of the fusion and fission cycle, respectively, at time 𝑡 , conditioned on the availability of 𝑁 v vesicles, 𝑁 + fusogens, and 𝑁 − fisogens. For concreteness, we will take the fusion and fission Markov cycles to have 4-states, ℵ+ = ℵ− = 4 (Fig. 1(b,c)). Figure 1: Nonequilibrum assembly of Golgi cisternae via fusion-fission. (a) Schematic showing Golgi cisternae of sizes 𝑀 1, 𝑀 2, 𝑀 3 maintained by the active fusion and fission of transport vesicles (blue filled circles) that form a continual net vectorial flux from the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) to the plasma membrane (PM), with influx rate 𝑣 and fusion-fission flux kernels 𝐾 fus , 𝐾 fis . (b) Active fusion described by a nonequilibrium 4-state Markov cycle, is activated by Rab-GTPase, tethering proteins and other fusogens such as v-SNARE at the transport vesicle and its cognate t-SNARE proteins at the cisterna that mark the docking site [1]. The external state |0⟩ represents the fusion-competent transport vesicle+fusogen. The internal states denoted by |𝑖 ⟩, 𝑖 = 1, . . . , 4, represent the chemical coordinate of the enzyme-fusogen-vesicle complex and the configurational coordinate of the cisternal membrane. Thus, |1⟩ =: (X, C 1), represents Rab-GDP-fusogen-vesicle and the undeformed cisternal membrane. Upon binding to GTP, |1⟩ → | 2⟩ =:(X-GTP, C 2), which represents Rab-GTP-fusogen-vesicle bound to the cisternal membrane at the docking site. This drives the formation of a membrane fusion intermediate, while undergoing a conformation change - |3⟩ =: (X ∗-GTP, C 3)- enroute to complete fusion |4⟩ =: (X ∗-GDP, C 4) and the conversion to the inactivated Rab-GDP which then unbinds from the cisternal membrane, thus completing the fusion cycle and making the fusogens available for another round of fusion. The backward transition rates (dashed line-arrows) are slower, the energy consuming rates and the consequent breaking of detailed balance ensure that the enzymatic cycle predominantly proceeds in the forward direction. On completion of this fusion cycle, the cisternal size increases by one unit. (c) Active fission described by a nonequilibrium 4-state Markov cycle, is activated by Arf-GTPase and other fisogens such as coat proteins COPII [1]. The external state |0⟩ denotes the recruitment of fisogens (dashed red line) proximal to the cisternal membrane. State |1⟩ =: (Y, C 1)represents Arf-GDP-fisogen and the undeformed cisternal membrane. Upon binding to GTP, |1⟩ → | 2⟩ =: (Y-GTP, C 2), which represents Arf-GTP-fisogens bound to the budding cisternal membrane and marks the exit site. This drives the formation of a complete bud, while undergoing a conformation change - |3⟩ =: (Y ∗-GTP, C 3) - enroute to complete fission |4⟩ =: (Y-GDP, C 4), and the conversion to the inactive GDP-bound form. The Y-GDP then unbinds from the vesiculated bud, and returns to |0⟩. At the end of this fission cycle, the cisternal size decreases by one unit. (d) An example of the profile of fusion (red line) and fission (green line) flux kernels as a function of 𝑀 , obtained from the microscopic model. By varying the microscopic rates, one gets three qualitatively distinct scenarios, corresponding to the topology of intersections of the fusion and fission kernels, which dictate the number of stable (filled circle) and unstable (open circle) fixed points (arrows denote the flows), see Fig. S4. (Inset) For de novo biogenesis the fusion flux rate should be larger than the fission rate at small 𝑀 .

¤𝑃 (𝑀, 𝑡 ) = 𝐾 fus (𝑀 − 1, ®𝜆 )𝑃 (𝑀 − 1, 𝑡 ) + 𝐾 fis (𝑀 + 1, ®𝜇 )𝑃 (𝑀 + 1, 𝑡 ) − ( 𝐾 fus (𝑀, ®𝜆 ) + 𝐾 fis (𝑀, ®𝜇 )) 𝑃 (𝑀, 𝑡 ) , (2) where the nonequilibrium fusion and fission flux kernels 𝐾 fus (𝑀, ®𝜆 ) and 𝐾 fis (𝑀, ®𝜇 ) are functions of cisternal size 𝑀 and parameter vectors ®𝜆 and ®𝜇 describe the microscopic transition rates of the fusion and fission processes, respectively. The flux kernels and the steady-state distribution of vesicles and the ±-species, are formally obtained from the steady state 4currents evaluated over the fusion and fission cycles (S1.1). Our task now is to provide tractable analytical forms for these fusion-fission kernels, based on a physico-chemical model of the fusion-fission cycles, Fig. 1(b). 

Physico-chemical basis for nonequilibrium flux kernels : We obtain 𝑃 𝑠𝑠 (𝑁 v, 𝑁 +), 𝑃 𝑠𝑠 (𝑁 − ) by assuming that the vesicle production, fusogen and fisogen availability are independent Poisson processes (see S1.2 for details). This helps us determine the probability of being in state |1⟩, when either the vesicles are fusion competent (Fig. 1(b)), or when the cisterna is primed for fission (Fig. 1(c)). In S1.2 we model the internal state dynamics in the fusion-fission cycles as a set of enzymatic chemical reactions with cooperativity, that describe the microscopic dynamics of fusogens and fisogens on the membrane, resulting in effective transition rates 𝑘 𝑖 𝑗 , ℓ 𝑖 𝑗 between the states, as shown in Fig. 1(b,c). We find that these transition rates depend on cisternal size 𝑀 on account of – (i) limited availability of fusogens/fisogens, tethering proteins etc. that mark the docking/exit sites at the cisterna; and (ii) dependence on composition (e.g., lipid specificity in a phase segregated domain) and mechanical properties (e.g., tension or curvature [32–39]) of the cisternal membrane, the latter either directly via mechanochemistry or actuated via a chemical pathway [40]. This model for the internal state dynamics, allows us to compute the dependence of 𝐾 fus (𝑀, ®𝜆 ) and 𝐾 fis (𝑀, ®𝜇 ) on 𝑀 (see Fig. 1(d) as example and S1.2 for details). 

Reduction to a low dimensional dynamical system : Taking the first moment of Eq. (2), and using a mean field decoupling 

(see S2), we find that the mean cisternal size, for which we continue to use the symbol 𝑀 , satisfies 

¤𝑀 = Kfus (𝑀, ®𝜆 ) − K fis (𝑀, ®𝜇 ) , (3) a nonlinear dynamical system, where Kfus/fis related to 𝐾 fus/fis , now has dimensions of mass current. While it might appear that there are many microscopic parameters that go to define the steady state currents (S1.1), we see that they can be combined into a probability of initiation of the rate limiting enzymatic reaction which depends on the enzyme availability and a net current associated with the enzyme catalysed reactions, leading to a dimensional reduction, a consequence of the timescales separation. This dimensional reduction can also be understood from the generic form of microscopic Kfus and Kfis – their asymptotic behaviour and the nature of intersections when plotted against 𝑀 . Owing to the limiting levels of the fusogens and fisogens, the availability of fusion-fission sites, and the dependence of the fusion-fission rates on membrane tension [32, 35], we find that the microscopic kernels increase with 𝑀 at first before saturating. Furthermore, varying the microscopic rates gives three qualitatively distinct scenarios associated with the topology of intersections of the fusion and fission kernels, which determines the number of stable and unstable fixed points, see Fig. 1(d) as example. This can be represented to arbitrary accuracy by the low dimensional forms (see S2, in particular Eqs. (S43),(S45)), 

Kfus = 𝑎 + 𝑏 𝑀 𝛼 

𝐶 1 + 𝑀 𝛼 Kfis = 𝑑 𝑀 𝛽 

𝐶 2 + 𝑀 𝛽 (4) Here, 𝑏, 𝑑 are the effective fusion (which includes the influx rate from the ER) and fission rates; 𝑎 (where 𝑎 /𝑏 ≪ 1) is the effective nucleation rate; and 𝐶 1, 𝐶 2 are the Hill saturation constants. The parameters 𝑏, 𝑑, 𝐶 1, 𝐶 2, are increasing functions of the availability of fusogens/fisogens [41], while the Hill-exponents 𝛼, 𝛽 , express the cooperativity of the fusogen and fisogens, respectively. This Michaelis-Menten form of the fusion-fission kernels has indeed been used in previous studies [12, 13]. The dimensional reduction strategy outlined here when extended to the case of multiple cisternae, leads to a tractable mean field dynamical system for the sizes of individual cisternae (see S2).

Our analysis of the nullcline (S4) reveals that regardless of the values of 𝛼 and 𝛽 (as long as they are positive definite), the topology of intersections (root structure) is maintained and always admits at most three fixed points. Thus without loss of generality, we may fix 𝛼 = 2 and 𝛽 = 1 (an argument for such cooperativity was also made in [2]). Using parameter values extracted from experiments (Table S1), sets the unit of time 𝑡 to be 1s. Glossary 1: Dynamical systems and Bifurcations [42–44] 

A continuous dynamical system can be represented by 

𝑑 X

𝑑𝑡 = f (X, 𝝀 , 𝑡 ) (5) where X ∈ R𝑛 is the state vector whose time ( 𝑡 ) evolution is given by a smooth vector field f : R𝑛 → R𝑛 along with 

𝝀 , the system parameters. The state space comprises all possible states X. Within this space, we identify invariant sets —such as fixed points (FP) given by f (X, 𝝀 , t) = 0) and periodic orbits (limit cycles, LC) — whose stability is defined by their basin of attraction , the set of initial conditions asymptotically approaching them. An invariant set, e.g. an isolated fixed point, is locally stable if its basin covers a local neighborhood, globally stable if it spans the entire state space, and unstable if generic nearby trajectories diverge. Consequently, we categorize the system’s behaviour in the parameter space 𝝀 into distinct phases: a stable phase (globally stable attractor), a multistable phase 

(multiple coexisting attractors), and a semistable phase (where the state space is partitioned into regions that either converge to a bounded solution or diverge to infinity). We call these different qualitative behaviours that the system admits as different solution classes. Bifurcations describe qualitative changes in a system’s behaviour, e.g., number or stability of equilibria/periodic orbits, as a parameter varies. Bifurcations can be local, driven by stability changes at a specific fixed point (detectable via linearization), or global, which reshape larger trajectories in phase space and cannot be detected by local analysis. We will be concerned with the following bifurcations in this work, 1. Saddle-Node (SN) bifurcation is a local bifurcation, that happens when two fixed points, that differ in stability along at least one direction, coalesce and vanish (or equivalently are created) as one varies the bifurcation parameter. It is accompanied by critical slowing down of system trajectory near the vanished fixed points, known as saddle-node ghost delay (Fig. 2(a)) [42]. 2. Hopf bifurcation manifests when a fixed point changes stability as a pair of complex-conjugate eigenvalues cross the imaginary axis, and it gives rise to a periodic orbit (limit cycle) locally (Fig. 2(b)) [42]. 3. Saddle-Node on an Invariant Circle (SNIC) bifurcation is a global bifurcation that involves a saddle-node bifurcation occurring directly on an invariant circle (Fig. 2(c)). At onset, the SNIC bifurcation produces a limit cycle of infinite period due to the saddle-node ghost; the period 𝜏 decreases continuously as the bifurcation parameter, 𝜇 moves away from criticality. Near the bifurcation point 𝜇 0, the period scales as: 

𝜏 ∼ 1/√𝜇 − 𝜇 0, a dependence inherited from the saddle-node ghost [42]. 4. Saddle-Node of limit cycles (SNLC) bifurcation is also a global bifurcation in which a stable limit cycle and an unstable limit cycle coalesce and annihilate as the bifurcation parameter varies, leaving the system to relax to a coexisting stable attractor ((Fig. 2(d)). In S5.3 and S8, we perform a detailed analysis of these bifurcations that demarcate the onset of the phases. The nature of these bifurcations determine the time scales of assembly of structures (Figs. 3(d, f) and 4(d)). .SNIC

Figure 3: Phase diagram for single cisterna. (a) Phase diagram in the 𝐶 1 (fusogen levels) - 𝑣 (influx rate) plane shows two stable phases – vesicle ( 𝑀 ≤ 1) and cisterna ( 𝑀 > 1), together with their coexistence ( bistable ). Cisternal size increases with influx rate 𝑣 (steady state sizes are shown as black contour lines) till there are no fixed points and the cisterna grows unboundedly. Here 𝐶 2 = 40 and 𝑎 0 = 0.05. The phase boundaries are derived analytically from Eq. (7) – the bistability region is obtained from the cubic discriminant Δ (thick green line), and the onset of cisterna formation (i.e. for a given 𝐶 1, there is a minimum value of influx rate 𝑣 required to form a stable cisterna, purple line), can be extracted from the coefficients of cubic polynomial Eq. (7) resulting from the transformation 𝑀 → 𝑀 + 1, Eq. (S58). (b) Phase diagram upon varying 𝐶 2 (fisogen levels) and 𝑣 with 𝐶 1 = 100 and 𝑎 0 = 0.05. The semistable phase corresponds to the situation where the system converges to a stable fixed point or grows unboundedly depending on the initial condition (Fig. S4(c)) and in most physical situations show up as a stable vesicle phase, ( 𝑀 < 1) for parameter values used here. (c) Stable fixed points of the single cisterna dynamics can also be interpreted as minima of a potential Φ(𝑀 ). (d) If fission is stopped, the cisterna swells linearly at a rate set by the peak influx rate, 𝑣 (𝑎 0 + 1) ≈ 𝑣 .(inset) On the other hand, if influx from the ER is stopped, the cisterna initially decays as 𝑀 (𝑡 ) ∼ 𝑀 (0) − 𝑑 𝑡 , and later as 𝑀 (𝑡 ) ∼ 𝑀 (0) exp (− 𝑑 /𝐶 2 𝑡 ) (parameter values 𝑣 = 0.5, 𝐶 1 = 100, 𝐶 2 = 40, 𝑎 0 = 0.05, 𝑑 = 1). (e) Dynamics of de novo cisternal formation shows a slow initial growth before it rapidly increases to the steady state value 𝑀 ∗, plotted here for 

𝑣 = 0.6, 𝐶 1 = 100 , 𝐶 2 = 40 , 𝑎 0 = 0.05. The slowing down is due to the presence of saddle-node ghost . Inset shows the non-monotonic dependence of the de novo Golgi formation time, 𝜏 = 𝜏 1 + 𝜏 2, at lower values of steady state cisternal size 𝑀 ∗ [48, 49]. Glossary 1 explains the nomenclature from dynamical systems theory. Fig. 3(a,b) and S4. These show a stable cisterna phase at intermediate values of the influx rate (keeping all other parameters fixed); this is consistent with the flux analysis done in [45]. The cisternal size increases with influx rate till it blows up when the fission flux rate goes beyond the fusion flux rate. The cubic polynomial loses (or gains) a pair of roots through a saddle-node (SN) bifurcation , leading to critical slowing down near the bifurcation point (see Glossary 1). This bifurcation changes the number of stable fixed points (1 ↔ 2), resulting in the creation or destruction of a stable equilibrium. In the case of two stable fixed points, the steady state cisternal size depends on initial conditions. The robustness of the phase diagram Fig. 3(a,b) to changes in the rest of the parameters is ensured by the property that Eq. (S57) represents a stable unfolding (see S4 and [46, 47]). Bounds on the robustness of the phase diagram to extrinsic and intrinsic noise are discussed in S7. Equation (6) can be interpreted as the overdamped dynamics of a particle in a potential Φ(𝑀 ) (Fig. 3(c)). The minimum of Φ(𝑀 ) locates the fixed point value of the cisternal size 𝑀 ∗, allowing us to address four relevant questions regarding the fate of a single cisterna – (i) What sets the cisternal size 𝑀 ∗ at steady state? This is primarily set by 

𝐶 1, 𝐶 2, the Hill saturation constants for fusion and fission flux kernels, which in turn is set by the levels of fusogens and fisogens (Fig. S5(e,g)) through the mean enzymatic rates 𝑘 𝑧 (see S2). Further, the steady state size will increase with the fusion rate, 𝑣 (Fig. 3(a)), and decrease with the fission rate 𝑑 . (ii) What happens when cisternal fission is stopped? This is commonly achieved by treatment with the drug Brefeldin-A [50–52]. We see that this leads to cisternal swelling that 7grows linearly at a rate set by the influx rate 𝑣 (Fig. 3(d)). (iii) What happens when the influx from the ER is stopped? 

This clearly leads to cisternal degradation, with a size that decays linearly at first followed by exponentially fast over a time scale set by the fission rate 𝑑 and the levels of the fisogen 𝐶 2 (Fig. 3(d,inset)). (iv) What sets the time scale for de novo cisternal formation? 

The time taken for de novo cisternal formation 𝜏 is determined by the sum of a slow lag time ( 𝜏 1), a consequence of the initial flatness of the potential Φ(𝑀 ) and the faster saturation time ( 𝜏 2), the characteristic time scale at the final steady state 𝑀 ∗ (given by the eigenvalue of the Jacobian of the dynamical system at 𝑀 ∗). Although the time scale can be computed from a straightforward numerical integration of Eq. (6), one may obtain an approximate analytical estimate from dynamical systems theory [42]. The slowing down of the trajectory is attributed to the presence of the saddle-node bifurcation that influences dynamical trajectories in the vicinity of the bifurcation point (known as a saddle-node ghost [42], details in S8.1). Using simplified forms for the fusion and unsaturated fission kernels (see S8.1), we obtain estimates for steady state cisternal size 𝑀 ∗ = (1 + 𝑎 0)𝐶 2 𝑣 /( 𝑑 − 𝑎 0 − 𝑎 0 𝑣 ) and 𝜏 = 𝜏 1 (𝛿 ) + 𝜏 2 (𝑀 ∗) ∼(𝐶 1 + 𝐶 2)/( 𝑑 − 𝑎 0 − 𝑎 0 𝑣 ) − 𝐶 2 𝑑 /( 𝑑 − 𝑎 0 − 𝑎 0 𝑣 )2 log (1 − 𝛿 ), in the vicinity 𝛿 = (𝑑 − 𝑎 0 𝑣 )𝐶 1/( 𝑎 0 𝐶 2 𝑣 ) of the bifurcation point. This provides a numerical estimate for the de novo cisterna formation time to be 5 − 10 mins (Fig. 3(e)) consistent with experiments on the recovery time of the cisterna upon Brefeldin-A washout [48, 53].  

> IV. NONEQUILIBRIUM ASSEMBLY OF MULTIPLE CISTERNAE

In extending our analysis to multiple cisternae (Fig. 1(a)), we immediately realise that the dynamical equations de-scribing cisternal size updates have to be non-local in time . This is because the fusion flux into cisterna 𝑖 from cisternae 

𝑖 − 1 and 𝑖 + 1, which goes to update the size of cisterna 𝑖 at time 𝑡 , depends on the fission dynamics of the donor cisternae at an earlier time. To avoid this, Refs. [12, 13, 54] consider the local dynamics of all membrane bound entities of sizes 

𝑀 ≥ 1 and numerically compute the size distribution at steady state, identifying the distribution at large 𝑀 with cister-nae. The price one pays is that the dynamical system is high (infinite) dimensional and has to be analysed numerically by statistically sampling over many vesicle trafficking networks (or initial conditions, as in [12]) with specified fusion and fission rules. Here, we will confront the nonlocal dynamics of the 𝑁 -cisternae system head-on, and attempt a simplifi-cation which will reduce to an 𝑁 -dimensional autonomous dynamical system. We will see that the nonlocality in time, together with the constraint that there is a fixed pool of cisternal specific fusogens and fisogens, result in inter-cisternal correlations in the fission and fusion flux kernels leading to complex flows in the 𝑁 -dimensional phase space. Details are presented in S5.1. We first recall that the intercisternal flux depends on the availability of cognate pairs of v-SNAREs and t-SNAREs at the donor and the target cisterna, respectively [53–63]. Consider the fission flux 𝐽 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 𝑖,𝑖 +1 from the donor cisterna 𝑖 to target cisternae 𝑖 + 1 at time 𝑡 . The appropriate v-SNAREs need to be transported on the transport vesicles destined to the target cisterna, leading to a depletion of the SNARE-pool and a reduced probability for subsequent fission events at 

𝑖 , unless replenished by fusion events at 𝑖 from 𝑖 + 1 [64]. This implies that the fission flux 𝐽 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 𝑖,𝑖 +1 at cisterna 𝑖 at time 𝑡 

depends on the fusion events at cisterna 𝑖 + 1 at earlier times within a transit time window , which in turn depend on the size 𝑀 𝑖 +1 (following the discussion in Sect. II). This contributes as a nonlocal kernel in time and cisternal index in the integro-differential equation for 𝑀 𝑖 (as in, for example, in Eq. (10) below), 

J 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 𝑖,𝑖 +1 (𝑀 𝑖 , 𝑀 𝑖 +1, 𝑡 ) ≡ 

∫

𝑑 𝑡 𝑤 𝑃 (𝑡 𝑤 ) 𝐽 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 𝑖,𝑖 +1 (𝑀 𝑖 , 𝑡 ; 𝑀 𝑖 +1, 𝑡 − 𝑡 𝑤 ) (8) where the intercisternal time window [27, 65, 66] is drawn from a probability distribution 𝑃 (𝑡 𝑤 ) which we take to have compact support (or having an exponential decay). Similarly, the fusion flux 𝐽 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 𝑖 +1,𝑖 at the target cisterna 𝑖 + 1 at time 𝑡 

depends on the fission events at the donor cisterna 𝑖 at earlier times within an intercisternal time window, which enters as a nonlocal kernel in time and cisternal index in the integro-differential equation for 𝑀 𝑖 +1. We may now use a mean field decoupling approximation (S5.1), 

J 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 𝑖,𝑖 +1 (𝑀 𝑖 , 𝑀 𝑖 +1, 𝑡 ) ∝ 𝑇 Kfis (𝑀 𝑖 (𝑡 )) K fus (𝑀 𝑖 +1 (𝑡 )) (9) that reduces the integro-differential equation to an ODE, where 𝑇 is the support of 𝑃 (𝑡 𝑤 ). This implicitly assumes that 

𝑇 is small and that the cisternal sizes do not vary much over this time scale. This allows us to prove the following proposition and corollary (proof in S5.2); for simplicity, we restrict ourselves to the 2-cisternae case, but the results hold more generally:

This unrestricted sign of the off-diagonal elements is supported by the finding that oversaturation of the trans-Golgi network with anterograde cargo reduces the efficiency and kinetics of retrograde transport. This, in turn, depletes early cisternae of the fusion and fission machinery essential for anterograde transport from the cis and medial Golgi [67, 68] — a bottleneck that arises because intercisternal flux depends critically on the availability of cognate SNARE pairs [64, 69]. We now propose an explicit realisation of intercisternal fluxes of the 2-cisternae system Eqs. (10),(11), that must satisfy the following reasonable criteria: (i) De novo biogenesis, implying presence of a nucleation seed for each cisterna. (ii) Mass action structure, implying J 𝑓 𝑖𝑠

(iv) In addition, we impose two physical conditions – for 𝑀 2 → 0, system should reduce to 1-cisterna dynamics and for 𝑀 1 → 0, 𝑀 2 should also vanish. The second condition ensures that the mass must accumulate in the first cisterna before the second cisterna starts growing and along with the first condition endows the two-cisternae system with a hierarchical structure, where the dynamics of second cisterna is conditioned on the fixed point structure of the first cisterna. Any system of equations with these properties will give rise to a qualitative similar phase diagram. With these considerations, together with the mean field decoupling Eq. A. Results for the 2-cisternae system 

As before, the steady state phase diagram of the 𝑁 -cisternae assembly can be obtained from an analysis of the root structure of the nullclines. For a general high-dimensional system, this is quite involved [72]; we will then have to rely on graphical and numerical analyses of the intersections of the nullclines and use bifurcation theory [42, 43, 73] to categorize the solutions (S5.3). In simple cases, such as for 2-cisternae, the nature and stability of the fixed points can be studied analytically using a two-dimensional version of the Routh-Hurwitz criterion [74], i.e. analysing the characteristic polynomial of the Jacobian matrix 𝐽 of the dynamical system evaluated at fixed points (𝑀 ∗

Figure 4: Phase diagram for the two cisternae system. (a) Numerical phase diagram for 2-cisternae system with stable fixed point (FP), Limit cycle (LC) and unbounded (Growing) states, in ( 𝑣, 𝑑 2) plane. Phase diagrams in ( 𝑣, 𝑑 12 )and ( 𝑣, 𝑑 21 ) planes are shown in Fig. S13. There are also regions with multistability, mixed phases where system converges to one phase or the other depending on the initial condition. Phase boundaries or bifurcation lines, can be obtained analytically from the behaviour of the eigenvalues of the Jacobian, and appear as saddle-node, SN bifurcations and Hopf bifurcations (see Glossary 1), as shown in Fig. S13. Rest of the parameter values are 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100, 

𝐶 22 = 20, 𝐶 12 = 20, 𝑑 12 = 2.5, 𝑑 21 = 0.2. Limit cycle solutions are categorized based on the phase difference between times series of 𝑀 1 and 𝑀 2 – in-phase limit cycle (LC 𝑖𝑛 : 0 − 60 deg.), and out-of-phase limit cycle (LC 𝑜𝑢𝑡 : 60 − 180 deg.). (b) In-phase oscillatory solutions represent spontaneous periodic dissolution and reformation of the two cisternae. (c) Out-of-phase oscillatory solutions can be associated with cisternal progression (CP) models and its variants based on the phase difference. (d) Time period of the limit cycle solution, 𝜏 OSC , in units of 𝑑 1 = 1𝑠 −1, varies with influx 𝑣 and is determined by the saddle-node delay due to the closest SNIC bifurcation. There are two saddle-node ghost delays for the oscillatory solutions in the system, one at small 𝑣 , when limit cycle solutions appear via SNIC bifurcation and the other at large 𝑣 , when limit cycle solutions disappear either via SNIC bifurcation or SNLC bifurcation (see Fig. S13(a,b,c)). (e) Variation of phase difference between times series of 𝑀 1 and 𝑀 2 with influx rate 𝑣 . At lower influx 𝑣 , the saddle-node ghost appear near 𝑀 1, 𝑀 2 ∼ 0, i.e. when both cisternae sizes are small (see bifurcation diagram Fig. S11(h) and Fig. S18(d)). Near this saddle-node ghost, the trajectories are slow and the phase lag, Δ𝜙 between the time series of 𝑀 1, 𝑀 2 does not change much – we therefore obtain in-phase oscillations. However, as 𝑣 increases, the trajectories come out of the ghost region and move fast – the phase lag between 𝑀 1, 𝑀 2 increases, which is further increased by the saddle-node ghost appearing at larger cisternal sizes, leading to out-of-phase oscillations (shaded region). The susceptibility 𝜕 𝑣 (Δ𝜙 ) peaks at each ghost (criticality) and is suppressed in the fast-transit region between them. (f) The phase difference increases with the retrograde rate 𝑑 21 , supporting observations that cisternal progression is accompanied by retrograde transport to recycle resident Golgi enzymes against the direction of cisternal flow [7, 9, 70, 71]. (g) Phase velocity, computed from the ratio of the phase difference 

Δ𝜙 and time period 𝜏 OSC , estimated to be v𝐶 𝑃 ∼ 0.1 − 0.2 stacks/min and agrees with the cisternal progression speed obtained from analyses of experiments [27]. For (d-g), parameter values are 𝑑 2 = 1.3, 𝑑 12 = 2.5. stable solution (a fixed point or a limit cycle) is accessible from given initial conditions (in the case of multiple locally stable solutions), requires numerical analysis of the asymptotic solution of the dynamical system ( asymptotic time domain analysis , S5.5). In this way, we demarcate the various phases of the 2-cisternae system (12),(13) in 𝑣, 𝑑 2, 𝑑 12 , 𝑑 21 space (Fig. 4(a) and Fig. S13). Stable fixed point solutions with two finite size cisternae ( 𝑀 1,2 > 𝑀 𝑡 ℎ , a threshold size), which we call the 2-cisternae phase, is accompanied by an in-flux, out-flux and intercisternal flux of cargo vesicles – we identify this phase with vesicular transport (VT). In addition, the dynamical system (12),(13), supports limit-cycle solutions with definite phase relations between 𝑀 1 and 𝑀 2. The in-phase oscillatory solutions correspond to the spontaneous periodic dissolution and reformation of the two cisternae. The out-of-phase oscillatory solutions correspond to a kind of A Results for the 2-cisternae system 10 travelling wave [122], which we identify with cisternal progression (CP). The phase velocity (stack speed) is computed from the time period of the oscillation 𝜏 OSC and phase difference Δ𝜙 , Fig. 4(f), and its numerical value is found to be comparable to the value of the cisternal drift estimated in [27]. Oscillatory solutions with intermediate phase difference can be identified with variants of the VT and CP models [5–9, 11, 70, 71, 75–79]. The phase boundaries can be obtained algebraically through a bifurcation analysis (S5.5) and are shown in Fig. S13(a,b,c). We note from Fig. 4(e,f) that the out-of-phase oscillations (CP phase) are separated from the in-phase oscillation as well from the stable cisternal phase by the presence of saddle-node ghosts, leading to a sharp rise of the susceptibility 𝜕 𝑣 (Δ𝜙 ) upon approaching each ghost at the phase boundaries. Hence, unless the system is fine tuned to operate near these boundaries, the limit cycle solutions generically remain in the CP phase. Returning to Fig. 4(a), we note that at large exit rate 𝑑 2, only cisterna-1 is formed (consistent with the analysis in [45]), and that the dynamical system has the same fixed point structure as the single cisterna case (see Fig. 3(b)), going from vesicle → bistable → stable single cisterna → semistable → unbounded growth, as the influx rate 𝑣 increases. Note that from Eq. (12), the outflux J 𝑓 𝑖𝑠  

> 12

from cisterna 1 increases with 𝑀 2. Hence, as 𝑣 is increased further, cisterna-1 grows unboundedly, until enough mass accumulates in the second cisterna, increasing the intercisternal flux from cisterna 1, leading to a stabilisation of the two-cisternae system via a Hopf bifurcation. Similarly, as we see in Fig. S13(b), the eventual size blowup at large 𝑣 is also stabilized via a Hopf bifurcation by increasing the outflux 𝑑 12 from cisterna-1 that goes to form the next cisterna-2. Fig. 4(a) also shows that for smaller terminal exit rates 𝑑 2, both cisternae are formed – on increasing 𝑣 , the vesicle phase transforms to a bistable two-cisternae phase. Note that similar to the single cisterna phase diagram (Fig. 3(b)), there are regions with multistability where the solutions are only locally stable and the system may either grow unboundedly or go to a stable fixed point, depending on the initial condition (see Fig. S13). On further increase of 𝑣 , the second cisterna blows up, and the system loses stability via Hopf bifurcation. However, if the saddle-node pair of fixed points in the bistable phase ( node-saddle-node ) happen to lie on a closed loop, then limit cycle (LC) solutions appear via a SNIC bifurcation (see Fig. S11(e,f)). Subsequently, limit cycle solutions disappear either via saddle-node of limit cycles (SNLC) or via another SNIC bifurcation (Fig. S13(a,b,c)). See Glossary 1 for dynamical systems nomenclature. For large 𝑣 , there are no fixed points, resulting in unbounded growth. The nullclines, bifurcations and the phase diagrams for the 2-cisternae system are presented in greater detail in S5.3 and S5.5. Note that in Fig. 4(a), there are three branches for 2-cisterna phase (shown in green) in (𝑣, 𝑑 2) plane – 𝑀 1 ≫ 𝑀 2 > 𝑀 𝑡 ℎ at low 𝑣 and high 𝑑 2, 𝑀 2 ≫ 𝑀 1 > 𝑀 𝑡 ℎ at low 𝑑 2 and high 𝑣 and 𝑀 1 ∼ 𝑀 2 > 𝑀 𝑡 ℎ at 𝑣 ∼ 𝑑 2. Similar arguments explain the phases in the (𝑣, 𝑑 12 ) and (𝑣, 𝑑 21 ) planes (Fig. S13(b) and Fig. S13(c)).  

> B. Response to systematic external and internal variations

We now ask how the different nonequilibrium phases, associated with stable fixed points or limit cycles, respond to external perturbations or systematic internal (cellular) variations. We first study the dynamical response of stable cisternal structures to sudden perturbations, such as (i) sudden change in influx 𝑣 , (ii) sudden change in intercisternal flux 𝑑 12 , and (iii) sudden change in outflux 𝑑 2, that may be triggered by biochemical agents or subjecting it to cellular stress. For this analysis, we continue to use Eqs. (12),(13) with initial conditions set at the steady state configurations. We consider the response of the stable cisternal phases to a sudden step or rectangular pulse, say in the influx rate 

𝑣 = 𝑣 0+𝑣 𝑝 W ( 𝑡 0, 𝑡 𝑤 ), where the perturbation is switched on at 𝑡 0 for a time 𝑡 𝑤 with unit amplitude W ( 𝑡 0, 𝑡 𝑤 ), and similarly for the other parameter rates. Such perturbations are easily achieved by treatment with specific drugs and subsequent washout. For instance, Brefeldin A (BFA), a fungal metabolite, prevents the formation of COPI vesicles [51, 53], thus affecting fission from the cis-Golgi cisterna and H89 that can inhibit COPII recruitment to ER exit sites [80], thus changing the influx rate 𝑣 . Ilimaquinone (IQ), a sea sponge metabolite, appears to specifically target the trans Golgi network (TGN) and induce rapid PKD (Protein Kinase D)-dependent fission reaction at TGN [78], thus enhancing the exit rate 𝑑 2. Figure 5(a-d) displays the dynamical responses of the stable 2-cisternae phase to these sudden perturbations. The dynamical response of the system to these perturbations provides insight into the form of the fusion-fission kernels and feedback structure that determine the solution space, and can be monitored using live-cell imaging. The dynamical response to sudden changes in the influx (Fig. 5(a)) is along expected lines. It is of interest, however, that a sudden negative pulse in the intercisternal flux 𝑑 12 (obtained, for instance, by reducing the fission from cisterna-1) leads to a swelling of cisterna-1 together with a decrease in the size of cisterna-2 (Fig. 5(b)). This has potential implications for the fate of trans-Golgi cisterna upon treatment with Brefeldin-A [50–52] which directly acts on the cis and medial Golgi cisternae. On the other hand, the response to a sudden step jump in the exit rate 𝑑 2 (Fig. 5(c,d)) is nontrivial and depends on the intercisternal rate 𝑑 21 and on the functional form of the intercisternal kernels. Experiments using IQ to disrupt the TGN often find that the preceding cisternae also get disrupted [78]. Our analysis would suggest that this would be the case when the retrograde rate 𝑑 21 is high. The time for complete fragmentation depends on the magnitude of the step jump in 𝑑 2. Further, we propose that in situations where the retrograde rate 𝑑 21 is low, the response would be closer to Fig. 5(c) where the outer cisterna disrupt while the inner cisterna is maintained at a larger size. Figure 5: Response to systematic perturbations. (a-d) Response of the stable cisternal phase, to a sudden step or rectangular pulse, as described in the text. (a) Dynamical response of the steady state to a sudden positive pulse in influx rate 𝑣 (inset). The sizes of both the cisternae grow linearly at first and decay after the pulse. Note that initially the rate of increase of 𝑀 1 is higher than 𝑀 2, and that after the pulse, 𝑀 1 reaches its steady state value quicker than 𝑀 2

(parameter values: 𝑑 12 = 2.5, 𝑑 21 = 0.2, 𝑑 2 = 2.2). (b) Dynamical response of the steady state to a sudden negative pulse in intercisternal transfer 𝑑 12 (inset). As the intercisternal flux from cisterna 1 to 2 goes down, cisterna 1 grows and cisterna 2 diminishes. Here again, after the pulse, 𝑀 1 reaches its steady state value quicker than 𝑀 2 (parameter values: 𝑣 = 2.3, 𝑑 21 = 0.2, 𝑑 2 = 2.2). (c) Dynamical response of the steady state to a sudden step jump in exit rate 𝑑 2

(inset), at small retrograde rate, 𝑑 21 (parameter values 𝑣 = 2.3, 𝑑 12 = 2.5, 𝑑 21 = 0.2). At a smaller step jump 𝑑 2 (inset, cyan dashed line), the size of the second cisterna decreases, leading to reduction in anterograde flux from the first cisterna, and a concomitant increase in the size of the first cisterna (dashed red line). At larger step jump 𝑑 2 (inset blue line), the second cisterna gets rapidly depleted, and the first cisterna grows unboundedly. (d) Dynamical response of the steady state to a sudden step jump in exit rate 𝑑 2 (inset), at larger retrograde rate, 𝑑 21 (parameter values 

𝑣 = 1.3, 𝑑 12 = 2, 𝑑 21 = 1.2). At a smaller step jump 𝑑 2 (inset, cyan dashed line), the cisternae resettle into a new stable configuration with a smaller size (dashed lines). At larger step jump 𝑑 2 (inset blue line), the second cisterna gets rapidly depleted, reducing the retrograde as well as the anterograde flux. However, in this regime, the net influx (J in + J fus 21 ) at the first cisterna is not sufficient to balance the net outflux ( J fis 12 + Jleak ) and the system collapses into a vesicle phase (solid lines). This can also be seen from the nullclines and flows in Fig. S12(d,e) (see S5.4 for details). Next we turn our attention to systematic periodic variations, where the nonequilibrium cisternal dynamics couples to internal cellular networks, such as the cell cycle, leading to strong cell-state dependent changes. The cell cycle is under the control of circadian clocks [81], via a variety of kinases (such as CK1 [82]) that might regulate the synthesis or influx from the ER [82]. This results in a periodic influx rate 𝑣 which, for the 2-cisternae dynamics, we model as 

¤𝑀 1 = 𝑠 𝐷 𝑠𝑞 (𝜔 𝐷 , 𝑡 ) + J 1 (𝑀 1, 𝑀 2) ¤𝑀 2 = J2 (𝑀 1, 𝑀 2) (14) where 𝑠 (𝑡 ) = 𝑠 𝐷 𝑠𝑞 (𝜔 𝐷 , 𝑡 ) is a periodic rectangular pulse with driving frequency 𝜔 𝐷 and strength 𝑠 𝐷 , and J1, J2 are given by R.H.S. of Eqs. (12),(13), respectively. To model the cell cycle, we take the time period of the driving signal (cell cycle time) to be ∼ 10 hrs, corresponding to 𝜔 𝐷 ∼ 0.00017 𝑟𝑎𝑑 𝑠 −1, and the driving strength such that the minimum influx approaches zero during the mitotic phase of the cell. Observationally, it is well established that Golgi cisternae in mammalian cells respond by disassembling at the onset of mitosis and reassembling at its completion [83, 84]. We first study the effect of the periodic influx on the stable 2-cisternae phase. In this case, provided the amplitude of the perturbation is large enough to cross the phase boundary to the vesicle or limit cycle phase, the cisternae simultaneously fragment and reassemble Fig. 6(a) at a period predominantly set by the cell cycle frequency, with nonlinearities exciting higher frequency modes. We next study the effect of the periodic influx on the in-phase limit cycle phase. We find that for small driving B Response to systematic external and internal variations 12

(c) Entrainment of the spontaneously oscillating system with the driving frequency showing the characteristic Arnold tongues. On the x-axis is the natural frequency, 𝜔 0 (i.e., system frequency without drive) in units of the driving frequency 𝜔 𝐷 , while the observed system frequency 𝜔 𝑜𝑏𝑠 in the presence of drive appears in the adjacent panel. We see from the Arnold tongue diagram that for non-zero driving strength, as we vary the system frequency 𝜔 0 by varying the retrograde rate 𝑑 21 , the observed frequency stays locked in 1 : 1 ratio with the driving frequency (yellow region) before moving to other frequency ratios at higher signal frequencies (blue and red strips). Parameter values are 𝑣 = 1.37, 𝑑 2 = 0.89, 𝑑 12 = 2.5, 𝑑 21 = 0.7 − 0.95, 𝑑 1 = 1 𝑠 −1, with the time period of the driving pulse signal = 10 ℎ𝑟𝑠 . At larger retrograde flux rate, the width of the first Arnold tongue shrinks, as shown by the arrow. This entrainment is observed even at small driving amplitudes ( 𝑠 𝐷 ∼ 0.01 − 0.05). amplitude, the observed frequency, 𝜔 𝑜𝑏𝑠 of the driven system becomes mode-locked to a rational multiple of the driving frequency, 𝜔 𝐷 , a phenomena known as entrainment (Fig. 6(b)). This locking persists over a finite range of detuning (or frequency mismatch) as we vary the system frequency 𝜔 0 by changing the retrograde rate 𝑑 21 , creating the regions known as Arnold Tongues (Fig. 6(c)) [42, 73]. Complete dissolution observed in Fig. 6(b) for limit cycle solutions occurs even when the driving amplitudes are small. This happens because the system spends significant time near the SN-ghost .It is observed that in mammalian tissue culture cells, but not in plant cells or in S. cerevisiae, protein transport from the ER is blocked during mitosis [85]. Thus plant cells and S. cerevisiae are not subject to periodic influx rate and so do not undergo fragmentation [85]. This suggests that the periodic dissolution and reformation of cisterna across the cell cycle should be associated with driving the stable 2-cisternae phase with a periodic influx, rather than the entrainment of the limit cycle phase. Nevertheless, it would be interesting to search for evidence of in-phase limit cycle solutions in the Golgi apparatus across the eukaryotic domain, or in synthetic realizations.  

> C. Robustness of the solution space

So far, our analysis has been mean field, where we have ignored the effect of noise, an inevitable presence in cellular systems. Cellular noise can arise from parametric noise, extrinsic noise (say, influx rate) and intrinsic noise (say, low copy number or chemical). An important requirement of organelle biogenesis in a living cell should be robustness under noise. In Sect. III and S4.1, we have discussed the structural stability of the single cisterna dynamical system Eq. (6) under parametric noise, i.e. states in the vicinity of a viable solution also converge to the same solution class or phenotype. The structural stability of the 2-cisternae dynamical system Eqs.(12), (13), although not as straightforward as the single cisterna, can be established numerically (see Fig. S14 and S6 for details) by checking that the number of distinct roots that the dynamical system admits matches its algebraic capacity (the generic number of roots that the system algebraically supports) using the Gr¨ obner basis [47, 86]. It has been suggested that the structural stability of a dynamical system ensures that the root structure of the system is robust to low amplitude extrinsic noise [87]. Changes in the root structure of the 1-cisterna and 2-cisternae systems in the presence of extrinsic multiplicative noise can be studied by extending the deterministic system to a Langevin system [88], for instance, by adding a multiplicative noise with noise strength √︁ 2𝜉 𝑖 (𝑀 𝑖 ), which is a smooth positive function solely dependent on the size of the cisterna 𝑖 . For the 1-cisterna case, taking the noise strength 𝜉 (𝑀 ) = 𝜖 𝑀 , which C Robustness of the solution space 13

𝐶 1 = 100, 𝑎 0 = 0.05, see Eq. (6)) and (b) two cisternae (parameter values 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100, 𝐶 22 = 20, 𝐶 12 = 20, 𝑑 12 = 3, 

𝑑 21 = 0, 𝑎 1 = 𝑎 2 = 0.05, see Eqs. (12),(13)). In (a), we have plotted the phase boundaries (thick lines) between stable cisterna-unbounded growth and vesicle-cisterna, of the deterministic system. See Fig. S9(b) for a more elaborate phase diagram. In (b), we have plotted the phase boundaries (thick lines) between vesicle and 1-cisterna, vesicle and 2-cisternae, 1-cisterna and 2-cisternae (here we do not differentiate between stable 2-cisternae and limit cycle), of the deterministic system. See Fig. S13(a) for a more elaborate phase diagram. We see that the mean field phases are robust under intrinsic noise, except at small values of influx rate, 𝑣 , in the vicinity of the bifurcations. In these regions, marked by red triangles, the cisternal sizes exhibit higher coefficient of variation (CV) suggesting the system is dominated by copy number variations (also see Fig. S16 and S7.2). would be the case if the stochastic addition and removal of vesicles are proportional to the cisternal size, we see that the nucleation rate is modified 𝑣 𝑎 0 → ( 𝑣 𝑎 0 − 𝜖 ). Although it can change the root structure, it also suggests that a non-zero nucleation rate 𝑣 𝑎 0 provides robustness against noise with strength √𝜖 𝑀 , with the bound for robustness on the noise strength being 𝜖 < 𝑎 0 𝑣 . For further details, see S7. The robustness of the solution space (phases) under intrinsic noise can be studied using stochastic Gillespie simula-tions [107] with propensities given by the effective fission and fusion rates. The asymptotic mean value from the stochastic trajectories can be used to generate a phase diagram that includes intrinsic noise. For single cisterna system, Fig. 7(a) shows that in the presence of intrinsic noise, the cisternal phase apparently occupies a larger region (light orange) in parameter space. However, analysis of the cisternal size shows a large coefficient of variation, the ratio of the standard deviation to the mean (CV > 1), suggesting that cisternal configurations are dominated by large copy number fluctua-tions, especially near the phase boundary adjoining the vesicle phase. Similar results are observed for the two cisternae system, Fig. 7(b). Further details in S7. 

V. DISCUSSION 

In this paper, we have presented a general nonequilibrium framework to study the establishment, control, robustness and dynamical response of a system of intracellular membrane bound organelles, such as the Golgi complex, which are subject to a nonequilibrium flux of material processes through synthesis, transport, fusion and fission. To do this, we have had to derive macroscopic fusion and fission flux kernels from microscopic considerations, that form an embedded feedback control system that ensure the assembly of multiple Golgi cisternae. This assembly, depending on the functional form of the flux kernels, admit different solution classes, which we identify with distinct phenotypic outcomes such as vesicular transport, cisternal progression, or the periodic dissolution into tiny vesicles. We have studied how these outcomes may be entrained with the cell cycle, giving rise to cell state dependent cisternal organisation. We have determined how the different phases of cisternal assembly dynamically respond to systematic perturbations, both external and internal. In future we propose to study how specific dynamical responses to experimentally realisable protocols, may enable the quantitative reconstruction of the fusion and fission kernels and its embedded feedback struc-ture, providing insights into the physical principles underlying cisternal assembly. We showed that the deduced effective fusion and fission kernels give rise to stable unfoldings that maintain the solution structure under perturbations with finite strength. We have further elaborated on this issue by subjecting the dynamical system model to intrinsic and extrinsic noise and derive bounds on the noise strengths for which system remains in the same solution class, hence a robust solution structure. There remain many open issues which we hope will be taken up in the future – 1. While the cellular dynamics discussed here, provides a framework to study its biogenesis and the control and 14 maintenance of cisternal size, it says says nothing about what controls cisternal number [89]. This could likely arise from additional cisternal attributes not included in our study of cisternal dynamics. Indeed cisternae are identified not only by their position relative to the ER and PM, but also by their lumenal or membranal chemical content, such as cisternal specific enzymes. We therefore proposed to extend the nonequilibrium framework developed here to include the dynamics of cisternal enzymes and their retention [89]. This may be done by coupling the mechanochemical enzymes involved in the fission-fusion cycles to the glycosylation enzymes, a preliminary attempt using a spatial model was made in [12]. 2. An important question in cell biology is how organelle size scales with cell size [4, 90]. In the context of our Golgi assembly model, this translates to asking which parameters of the model depend on cell size and how. The rate of influx from the ER and the fission and fusion parameters, such as levels of fisogens and fusogens, that characterise the nonequilibrium cycles clearly depend on ploidy and general metabolic activity and must therefore scale with cell size [4]. 3. Since finite size cellular structures are a consequence of the nonequilibrium dynamics and feedback control, we may ask for the thermodynamic cost in creating and maintaining stable cisternae [91]. Finally, we believe our model for the biogenesis, maintenance and control of the Golgi cisternae can be extended to other intracellular organelles [90]: open, nonconserved systems like the Golgi, such as the Endosomal and Lysosomal systems, and closed, conserved systems such as the Mitochondrial system.

Appendix S1: Master equation for de novo assembly of Golgi cisternae S1.1. Detailed derivation for n-enzyme model for Master equation 

Consider a cisterna of size 𝑀 , with 𝑁 + fusion enzymes and 𝑁 − fission enzymes. The fusion and fission cycles consist of enzyme-cisternal membrane complex going over a four state Markov cycle, in the end of which size of the cisterna increases or decreases by one unit of vesicle. The complete state space for this process can be represented by (𝑀, {𝑖 𝑟 }, {𝑘 𝑠 }, 𝑡 ),

𝑟 ∈ ( 1, . . . , 𝑁 +) and 𝑠 ∈ ( 1, . . . , 𝑁 − ) where 𝑟, 𝑠 represent the 𝑟 th and the 𝑠 th , fusion and fission cycles, respectively. S1.2. Physico-chemical basis for nonequilibrium flux kernels 

In our analysis we consider a finite cisternal pool of fusogens and fisogens, set by the expression levels of the cell. This constraint can be imposed in different ways, e.g., (i) Constant rate ensemble – In this ensemble, fusogens/fisogens are assumed to be available at a constant rate. Specifically, the rate of fusogen/fisogen arrival per unit time is a strict constant 𝑟 0, described by 𝑃 (𝑟 ) = 𝛿 (𝑟 − 𝑟 0).Because the subunits arrive independently, the probability of observing 𝑛 arrivals within a given time window follows a Poisson distribution [88]. (ii) Finite pool ensemble – In this ensemble, we assume that at any given time, the cell contains a finite total number of fusogens/fisogens, 𝑁 𝑡𝑜𝑡 𝑎𝑙 . Consequently, each time an enzyme engages in a fusion, fission or transport event, one fewer fusion/fission enzyme remains available at the cisterna. If the size of the Golgi is 𝑀 , the number of available free enzymes is dictated by a function P𝑠 (𝑀 ), which represents the number of independent active patches at the cisternal membrane. The number of free enzymes available is therefore 𝑚 = 𝑁 𝑡𝑜𝑡 𝑎𝑙 − P 𝑠 (𝑀 ). The probability of observing exactly 𝑚 free fusogens/fisogens (which governs the fluctuating arrival rate) follows a binomial distribution, given by 

𝑃 (𝑚 ) =

𝑁 𝑡𝑜𝑡 𝑎𝑙 

𝑚 



𝑝 𝑚 (1 − 𝑝 )𝑁 𝑡𝑜𝑡 𝑎𝑙 −𝑚 (S13) where 𝑝 is the probability that a given enzyme is currently free in the pool rather than bound to the Golgi, determined by the specific binding and unbinding rates of the system [16, 17]. Here, we choose to work with the constant rate ensemble. However, the methods used in this work (for instance, steps for reduction to a lower dimensional system, see S2) can be used for other ensembles as well. Now, to construct the fusion-fission flux kernels from Eqs.(S10),(S11),(S12), we would need the steady state prob-abilities 𝑃 𝑠𝑠 (𝑁 v, 𝑁 ±), the conditional probability in a given internal state 𝑃 𝑠𝑠 (𝑖 |𝑀, 𝑁 v, 𝑁 ±), and the transition rates S1.2 Physico-chemical basis for nonequilibrium flux kernels 21

Figure S1: (a) Material flux through multiple cisternae from Endoplasmic reticulum (ER) to plasma membrane (PM). Vesicle transfer from ER to cis-Golgi is facilitated by COPII whereas intercisternal vesicle trasfer is facilitated by COPI protein [22]. (b) When summed over fisogens, fusogens and internal cycle, we get master equation in terms of cisternal size dependent cycle current, where fusion cycle increases cisternal size and fission cycle decreases cisternal size (see chemical master equation, Eq.(S12)). Each ellipse represents sum over four states of fusion and fission cycles. (c) Four-state Rab-GTPase activation fusion cycle. The input to this Markov cycle is a flux of vesicle (yellow circle) and GTP. The forward transitions are represented by full line-arrows, while the reverse arrows are shown as dashed line-arrows. The nonequilibrium cycle clearly supports a current, which results in the increase of cisternal size by one unit. (d) Four-state Arf-GTPase activation fission cycle. The input to this Markov cycle is a flux of GTP, while the corresponding output is a flux of GDP, P 𝑖 , and a vesicle. As mentioned above, we will assume that fusion and fission are independent events and hence, the distributions of fisogens and fusogens are also independent. We take up the fusion and fission flux kernels separately.

and we can drop the combinatorial factor above. As the probability of the vesicle being in state |0⟩ is conditioned on the availability of Rab-GDP, the sum over vesicle number 𝑁 v

in Eq. (S12) starts from 𝑁 +. Furthermore, the steady state distribution of the fusogens (Rab-GDP), 𝑃 𝑠𝑠 (𝑁 +) is derived from a Poisson process with rate 𝑘 𝑧 ,

𝑃 𝑠𝑠 (𝑁 +) ≈ exp (− 𝑘 𝑧 ) (𝑘 𝑧 )𝑁 +

𝑁 +! (S16) Here, 𝑘 𝑧 is the Poisson rate of availability of fusion enzymes (fusogens), which sets 𝑁 +. These fusion-competent vesicles in state |0⟩ then enter the fusion cycle, characterized here by a 4-state Markov process (see Fig.S1(c)). The states are denoted by (X, C), the chemical coordinate of the enzyme-vesicle complex and the configurational coordinate of the cisternal membrane. Thus, the state |1⟩ =: (X, C 1), where X is the fusion competent vesicle together with the SNARE proteins[1] and C 1 is the undeformed cisternal membrane. The cytoplasmic Rab-GDP gets converted to a membrane bound Rab-GTP by a GEF (Guanine nucleotide exchange factor) and vice-versa by a GAP (GTPase activating protein). Upon binding to GTP via a GEF, state |1⟩ gets converted with rate 𝑘 12 to state |2⟩ =: (X-GTP, C 2), which is membrane bound Rab-GTP + membrane inserted SNARE proteins + vesicle bound to cisternal membrane at specific functional domains where several factors – including t-SNAREs, tethering proteins, and Rab GTPases – work together to capture the vesicle [31], marking the “docking sites” at the cisterna. These activated fusogens then drive the formation of a membrane fusion pore with rate 𝑘 23 , while undergoing a conformation change - state |3⟩ =: (X ∗-GTP, C 3) - enroute to complete fusion state |4⟩ =: (X ∗-GDP, C 4) with rate 𝑘 34 and the conversion to the inactivated Rab-GDP which then unbinds from the cisternal membrane, thus completing the fusion cycle and making the fusogens available for another round of fusion. The backward transition rates are slower, the energy consuming rates and the consequent breaking of detailed balance ensures that the enzymatic cycle predominantly proceeds in the forward direction. On completion of this fusion cycle, lipids and lumenal material are pumped into the cisterna, and the cisternal size increases by one unit. The above description of the mechanical states of the cisternal membrane is, of course, a discrete approximation of a continuous membrane deformation and the transition rates depend on mechanical properties of the cisternal membrane such as membrane tension (or curvature) [32, 34]. Formally, this four-state fusion cycle can be represented by a continuous-time Markov chain (CTMC)[102]. Now, as per the master equation Eq.(S12), we need to specify the dependence of the microscopic current on the number of fusion enzymes. As suggested above (and seen in Eq.(S17)), some of the transition rates 𝑘 𝑖 𝑗 depend on the cisternal size 𝑀 . This arises from two main sources: the first one is the availability of fusogens and cisternal docking sites for the fusion primed vesicles. For instance, the probability of being in state |1⟩ given by Eq.(S15) is conditioned on the availability of fusogens, with 𝑘 𝑧 being the Poisson rate of finding fusogens. The transition rate 𝑘 12 is taken to be proportional to the number of “docking sites” 𝑚 . Also, we have assumed that the probability of finding 𝑚 “docking sites” on a cisterna of size 𝑀 for a given 𝑘 number of vesicles, 𝑃 𝑑 (𝑚 |𝑀, 𝑘 ), is generated via a Poisson point process [103], i.e.,

Fission flux kernel : The analysis of the fission cycle follows along similar lines (see Fig. S1(d)). The fission process is catalysed by fisogens such as Arf-proteins, a GTPase enzyme cycle, which we describe by a 4-state Markov process [102, 105]. The external state |0⟩ is a sequestration of fisogens (dashed red line) proximal to the cisternal membrane exit site. State |1⟩ =: (Y, C 1) represents Arf-GDP-fisogen and the undeformed cisternal membrane. Upon binding to GTP, 

|1⟩ → | 2⟩ =: (Y-GTP, C 2), which represents Arf-GTP-fisogens bound to a budding cisternal membrane at specialized domains where molecular machinery – such as Arf-GTPase, GEFs, and coat proteins – concentrate to deform the lipid bilayer [23, 24], marking the “exit/budding sites” on the cisterna. This drives the formation of a complete bud, while undergoing a conformation change - |3⟩ =: (Y ∗-GTP, C 3) - enroute to complete fission |4⟩ =: (Y-GDP, C 4), and the conversion to the inactive GDP-bound form. The Y-GDP then unbinds from the vesiculated bud, and returns to |1⟩ and then to state |0⟩. It can be represented by the reaction scheme, S1.3 Dynamics of the mean cisternal size 25

Appendix S2: Reduction to a low dimensional dynamical system 

Given that the master equation in the above section consists of highly non-linear terms, it becomes analytically intractable [106]. Hence, extracting a macroscopic understanding from it can be arduous. We will address this issue through a culmination of three methods: (i) a graphical method where we look at the fixed points of the mean field equations (ii) a phase diagram based method where we analyse the solution classes of the master equation in the space of its parameters using simulations (Gillespie algorithm) and map it to the space of macroscopic parameter, of a plausible macroscopic model. The resulting comparison confirms or invalidates the assumed macroscopic model. (iii) an algebraic method where we construct an effective function (of cisternal size as well as the fusogens/fisogens) from different parts of the sum in Eqs. (S27),(S28) containing steady state current along with enzyme and site availabilities. 

S2.1. Dimensional reduction through parsimony 

Eqs (S19), (S21) provide us with explicit but cumbersome formulae for computing fusion-fission kernels, assuming internal states equilibrate much faster than the dynamics of cisternal size. We compute the current over fusion and fission cycles and then plug it into the stochastic Master equation Eq. (S12). Taking the first moment of the resulting equation, and using a mean field decoupling , we derive Eqs.(S27),(S28) and find that the mean cisternal size, for which we continue to use the symbol 𝑀 satisfies the equation (see S1.3), 

𝑑𝑀 𝑑𝑡 = K 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 (𝑀, ®𝜆 ) − K 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 (𝑀, ®𝜇 ) (S29) where K 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 / 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 now has dimensions of a mass current. This is similar to state-dependent assembly-disassembly rates in the case of microtubule formation, discussed in [15]. This represents a nonlinear dynamical system for 𝑀 , with the microscopic fusion flux kernel described by 15 model parameters and the microscopic fission flux kernel described by 14 model parameters (Table S1) - a total of 29 parameters! To reduce this high dimensional parameter space to a low dimensional one, it suffices to understand the generic form of K 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 and K 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 , their asymptotic behaviour and the nature of intersections when plotted against 𝑀 . Taking into consideration the limiting values of the fusogens and fisogens, the availability of fusion-fission sites (which scale with 𝑀 )and the dependence of the fusion-fission rates on membrane tension [32, 35], we expect that the microscopic kernels are increasing functions of 𝑀 and must asymptote to a constant. Figure S4: Intersections of mean fusion and fission kernels. (a)-(d) Mean fission and fusion flux kernel can be obtained by computing the mean field equations (see S1.3, S2.1 and Eqs.(S27),(S28)). Varying the parameters in Eqs.(S27),(S28), one gets the scenarios – where fission and fusion kernels have one (a,b) or three (d) intersections for 

(𝑘 𝑝 ) 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 > (𝑘 𝑝 ) 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 or two (c) intersections for (𝑘 𝑝 ) 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 ∼ ( 𝑘 𝑝 ) 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 or zero (e) intersections. Varying microscopic rates changes number of stable (filled circle) and unstable (open circle) fixed points. This result can be used to map the microscopic model to a dynamical system problem. Default values of the parameters are, (𝑘 𝑝 ) 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 ∼ 0.05 − 0.1, 

(𝑘 𝑝 ) 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 ∼ 0.1, (𝑘 𝑧 ) 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 ∼ 1 − 4, (𝑘 𝑧 ) 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 ∼ 1, 𝑙 023 ∼ 1 − 10 , 𝑘 034 ∼ 1 − 10, 𝛾 0 ∼ 5000 𝐾 𝐵 𝑇 /𝜇 m2, 𝐶 𝛾 ∼ 5000 𝐾 𝐵 𝑇 /𝜇 m2, see Table S1. (f) If the fission events are more cooperative than the fusion events, the largest positive root is an unstable one, and the system grows unboundely. It can’t ensure stable biogenesis for generic parameters. This is verified by explicitly plotting the kernels with physically reasonable choices of microscopic parameter values. The summation in Eqs.(S27),(S28), can be carried out numerically and mean fission and fusion kernels can be plotted. Fig.S4(a)-(f) illustrate various intersections of these mean microscopic kernels as one varies the kinetic parameters, giving rise to stable and unstable fixed points. We observe that for a wide range of physical parameters, fission and fusion kernels have zero, one, two or three intersections. Varying microscopic rates changes number of stable (filled circle) and unstable (open circle) fixed points. Assuming enzyme kinetics, such a behaviour can be approximated to arbitrary accuracy by the low dimensional forms 

K 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 = 𝑣 



𝑎 0 + 𝑀 𝛼 

𝐶 1 + 𝑀 𝛼 



(S30) 

K 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 = 𝑑 𝑀 𝛽 

𝐶 2 + 𝑀 𝛽 (S31) The fusion flux kernel, is written in terms of influx rate , 𝑣 and effective nucleation constant , 𝑎 0 while the fission flux kernel is written in terms of the effective peak fission rate , 𝑑 . The parameters 𝐶 1, 𝐶 2 represent the levels of fusogens and fisogens or Hill saturation constants , respectively, while the Hill-exponents 𝛼, 𝛽 , specify the cooperativity of the fusogen and fisogens. We have taken the microscopic rates (𝑘 𝑝 ) 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 > (𝑘 𝑝 ) 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 and (𝑘 𝑧 ) 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 > (𝑘 𝑧 ) 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 , i.e., the fusion events are more cooperative than the fission events, also evident in Fig. S4(a)-(e). Such cooperativity can arise through the following mechanisms: (i) Enzyme allostery : It is molecular and structural in nature. For example, Arf-GTP recruits the the COPI coat subunits and they exhibit lateral protein-protein cooperativity. The initial recruitment of a coatomer provides binding interfaces that thermodynamically enhance the recruitment of adjacent subunits [24] via elastic interactions. (ii) Substrate allostery : The cisternal membrane undergoes localized physical alterations that lower the energy barrier for subsequent events. For example, The initial binding of COPI coatomers induces local membrane bending. This S2.1 Dimensional reduction through parsimony 28 locally curved lipid membrane physically matches the inherent curvature of the COPI complex, acting as a highly favorable physical ”substrate” that thermodynamically enhances the recruitment of subsequent coat subunits to continue the invagination [32]. (iii) Enhanced availability due to a prior proximal event : This is an example of dynamic cooperativity. For example, in a Rab cascade, membrane-bound Rab GTPases recruit GEFs to rapidly activate more Rabs [63]. This cascading expansion recruits tethers that cluster resident t-SNAREs together, creating a hyper-reactive patch that ensures vesicle fusion [60]. (iv) Trans-membrane cooperativity : Unlike fission, which occurs on a single continuous membrane, fusion requires the cooperative coupling of enzymes on the incoming vesicle (v-SNAREs) and the cisternal membrane (t-SNAREs) [60]. Based on the above, we argue that fusion is functionally more cooperative because it benefits from both dynamic coop-erativity (Rab cascades [63]) and robust inter-membrane scaffolding (trans-SNARE cooperativity [60]). This continuous expansion is only terminated by delayed Rab-GAP feedback [63]. In contrast, fission is geometrically self-limiting; al-though COPI assembly exhibits initial substrate allostery, the mature bud’s extreme curvature acts as a strict physical timer, triggering Arf-GAP1 to abruptly halt polymerization [24, 37, 62]. We will see in S4.1 that this is a necessary condition for de novo biogenesis [2]. This Michaelis-Menten (and Hill) form of the fusion-fission kernels has been used in previous studies [12, 13]. The dimensional reduction strategy outlined here (from 29 parameters to 7), and extended to many cisternae, makes the form of the fusion-fission kernels tractable, and leads to a low dimensional dynamical system for the sizes (masses) of the individual cisternae.  

> S2.2. Dimensional reduction through simulating stochastic trajectories

Another way to construct an effective description of the rates in the master equation is via simulating the trajectories for the master equation and then analysing the asymptotic behaviour ( asymptotic time domain analysis ). We use the Gillespie algorithm [107] to compute the system trajectories and then calculate the average over these stochastic trajectories at large time (steady-state average). Hence, we demarcate the solutions in the parameter space of the microscopic system based on the steady-state behaviour and construct a phase diagram. Then, assuming a reliable macroscopic model, one can project this phase diagram to the space of parameters in this macroscopic model. We then compare the projected phase diagram to the phase diagram for the macroscopic model. The map between single cisterna microscopic model and a macroscopic Hill-type model is given below (Eq. (S33)).

S2.3. An algebraic criterion for dimensional reduction 

Here, we present an algebraic method to arrive at the macroscopic rates from the microscopic model. We derive the functional form that the steady state microscopic current takes for various functional forms of microscopic transition rates in the Markov cycles and the probability distributions for enzyme/site availabilities. To this end, we derive the functional form for the steady state current in a Markov cycle. 

Steady state current in a Markov cycle: S2.3 An algebraic criterion for dimensional reduction 30 We will compute steady-state current in a general Markov cycle with 3 and 4 nodes for any given transition rates. Then we will assume specific cisternal size dependencies for the transition rates to compute the effective dependence of this current on the cisternal size, 𝑀 .

3-nodes: 

Consider a Markov system with three nodes (Fig.S6(a)), with rates given by (𝑎, 𝑎 ′, 𝑏, 𝑏 ′, 𝑐, 𝑐 ′), where unprimed indices correspond to forward transitions and primed indices correspond to reverse transitions. With the transition rate matrix [102],

Note that if the tension dependence of microscopic rates are of the Hill or Michaelis-Menten type and if the dynamic range 𝐶 𝛾 is large, membrane tension is a slowly varying function of the cisternal size. Hence, the Hill-exponent and saturation constant are largely determined by the site availability factor, whereas the internal cycle contributes to the rate in the numerator (limited by the smallest forward rate). Furthermore, for more complicated polynomial or Hill-kind dependencies of microscopic rates on the cisternal size (see discussion on the computation of current in a Markov cycle above), fission/fusion kernel can be approximated by a product of two Hill functions with different exponents, summed over the site and enzyme probabilities (i.e., over 𝑚, 𝑛 ). This sum can be further approximated by a Hill function.
