Figure S7: (a)-(c) Fission and fusion kernels when domain budding and flattening rates are given via Helfrich Hamiltonian [108] with bending rigidities, 𝜅 𝐵 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 = 2000 , 𝜅 𝐵 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 = 1950, membrane stretching modulus, 𝑘 𝑠 = 1, and membrane rest tension, 𝛾 0 = 2000, in the units of 𝑘 𝐵 𝑇 . Varying the parameters, one gets different scenarios, where fission and fusion kernels have one, or more intersections, changing the number of stable and unstable fixed points. With this model, size dependence in fusion kernel is not dominated by the site availability, but the tension dependence of the fusion-cycle rates; monotonicity of the fusion kernel is lost, however the fixed point structure remains the same for one cisterna.  

> S2.4. Variations in the form of kernels do not change the topology of fixed points

The functional form of microscopic mean kernels would change if the following changes : (a) The functional dependence of the enzyme availability (assumed to be a Poisson process here) changes. It can happen if the Poisson rate changes, or the underlying process could itself be a non-Poisson process. For instance, a finite pool ensemble [16, 17] would be give non-Poissonian availability of active fusion/fission machineries. (b) The availability of the “docking” and/or “exit” site has a different dependence (say, stronger or weaker) on the cisterna size. (c) Transition rates in 4-state Markov cycle, have different dependencies on size and tension. We will explore the third point now. Suppose that the size dependence in the fusion kernel is not dominated by the site availability but the tension depen-dence of the fusion-cycle transition rate. If these transition rates are non-monotonic in cisternal size, kernels will be no more monotonically increasing function of cisternal size. For instance, when the budding and the flattening rates are given by Helfrich free energy [108].          

> H ( 𝛽 )=𝜅 𝐵 (√︁ 𝛽 −1)2+𝛾 𝛽 (S47)

where 𝛾 = 𝛾 0 − 𝑘 𝑠 (𝑀 − 𝑀 0), is the Golgi tension; 𝛾 0, 𝑀 0 are the rest tension and size and 𝑘 𝑠 is the membrane stretching modulus for the Golgi cisterna, 𝜅 𝐵 is the bending rigidity and 𝛽 is the budding parameter (0 ≤ 𝛽 ≤ 1), 1 being a fully budded domain and 0 being a flattened domain, the line tension is assumed to be zero [33]. We choose the bending rigidities for the fusion and fission processes to be ( 𝜅 𝐵 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 = 1950 , 𝜅 𝐵 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 = 2000 , 𝑘 𝑠 = 1, 𝛾 0 = 2000, in the units of 𝐾 𝐵 𝑇 [34]). Domain budding and flattening events correspond to transitions |3⟩ ⇋ |4⟩ for fusion and |3⟩ ⇋ |2⟩ for fission. With these parameter values, the budding and flattening rates, given by Kramer’s barrier [34, 88] for Helfrich free energy [108], change its monotonicity in the relevant physical range of the cisternal size and the obtained mean fusion and fission kernels are non-monotonic. However, the topology of the fixed points remains the same for one cisterna (Fig.S7). We will explore the consequences of such kernels in future work. 

> Appendix S3: Stochastic master equation for two cisternae

In this section, we sketch how to extend the stochastic master equation Eq. (S12), with microscopic parameters for two and more cisternae. This essentially involves specifying how material is transferred from cisterna 1 to 2 and vice versa. We discuss the following two schemes that precisely address this, (a) Direct scheme : vesicles are directly transferred from cisterna 1 to 2 via COPI proteins, and this material transfer from cisterna 1 to cisterna 2 happens within a given intercisternal time 𝑇 12 , at the end of which cisternal sizes are updated. In the Gillespie code, this can be implemented as follows: Suppose the sizes of the cisterna 1 and 2 are 𝑀 1 and 𝑀 2 respectively. Given the values of all the microscopic rates, we need to compute the probability of, say, 𝑛 number of vesicles fissing out at cisterna 1, and then fusing to cisterna 2. This involves finding 𝑛 fission enzymes, then at least 𝑛 exit sites at cisterna 1, and then finding at least 

𝑛 docking sites at cisterna 2. This multiplied by the intercisternal time 𝑇 12 and the probability of finding 𝑛 COPI 34

Appendix S4: Detailed dynamical system analysis of a single cisterna 

With various methods outlined in S2, the deterministic mean dynamics for the cisternal size 𝑀 can be written as

The parameters 𝐶 1, 𝐶 2 represent the saturation constants for the fusion and fission kernels, respectively, while the Hill-exponents 𝛼, 𝛽 , define the cooperativity of the fusion and fission processes. The influx rate 𝑣 and the nucleation constant, 

𝑎 0 > 0 ensure that there is a nucleation seed for the cisterna. In the main text, we have converted this equation into dimensionless form by setting 𝑑 = 1. Now, we study the root structure of this dynamical system. 

S4.1. Number of positive roots 

Given the Hill-type kernels, that we have assumed above, one can have at most three fixed points, regardless of the value of Hill exponents. To see this, we notice that the fixed point of the above equation are given by 

𝐶 1𝐶 2𝑎 0𝑣 + 𝐶 1 (𝑎 0𝑣 − 𝑑 )𝑀 𝛽 + 𝐶 2 (𝑣 + 𝑎 0𝑣 )𝑀 𝛼 + ( 𝑣 + 𝑎 0𝑣 − 𝑑 )𝑀 𝛼 +𝛽 = 0 (S53) 

The above polynomial system can have at most three sign changes in its coefficients, and hence at maximum three positive roots, by Descartes’ rule of signs [109, 110] and exactly that many positive roots if all roots are real. Various scenarios for how this plays out for 𝛼 = 2, 𝛽 = 1 are shown in Table S2. Since the number of roots does not depend on the values of 𝛼, 𝛽 ; we may fix 𝛼 = 2, 𝛽 = 1 from here onwards without loss of generality, resulting in the dynamical equation, 

𝑑𝑀 𝑑𝑡 = 𝑣 



𝑎 0 + 𝑀 2

𝐶 1 + 𝑀 2



−

 𝑑 𝑀 𝐶 2 + 𝑀 



(S54) 

However, it should be noted that the nature of roots depends on the values of 𝛼 and 𝛽 . Eq.(S53) with 𝛼 = 2, 𝛽 = 1gives 

𝐶 1𝐶 2𝑎 0𝑣 + 𝐶 1 (𝑎 0𝑣 − 𝑑 ) 𝑀 + 𝐶 2 (𝑣 + 𝑎 0𝑣 ) 𝑀 2 + ( 𝑣 + 𝑎 0𝑣 − 𝑑 ) 𝑀 3 = 0 (S55) S4.1 Number of positive roots 36

Table S2: Root structure of cubic polynomial Eq.(S53): Descartes’ rule of signs [109] suggests that number of positive roots of a polynomial is either equal to the number of sign changes in the polynomial coefficients or less by an even number. A cubic discriminant gives the maximum number of real roots for a cubic polynomial ( Δ > 0 for three real roots and Δ < 0 for one). Combined with the fact that only cubic and linear term of polynomial Eq.(S53) can change sign, we can enumerate number of roots as we vary the parameters (Fig.S4(a-e)). This can also be deduced from say, Cardano’s formula [110] for cubic polynomials, but the method used here has wider applicability, including for higher order polynomials. Now, interchanging 𝛼 and 𝛽 ( 𝛼 = 1, 𝛽 = 2) in the above equation changes the order of the coefficients 

𝐶 1𝐶 2𝑎 0𝑣 + 𝐶 2 (𝑣 + 𝑎 0𝑣 ) 𝑀 + 𝐶 1 (𝑎 0𝑣 − 𝑑 ) 𝑀 2 + ( 𝑣 + 𝑎 0𝑣 − 𝑑 ) 𝑀 3 = 0 (S56) Given that the parameters are positive valued, in Eq.(S55), the order of sign changes for the coefficient of the polynomial is (+ , ±, +, ±) allowing maximum three sign changes and hence maximum three positive roots. However, in Eq.(S56), the order of sign changes for the coefficient of the polynomial Eq.(S56) is (+ , +, ±, ±) allowing for a maximum of two sign changes and hence a maximum of two positive roots. So, for 𝑎 0 > 0, 𝑣 > 0, Eq.(S56) has at most two positive roots and since Eq. (S52) corresponds to a positive system (which is ensured by the flows of the dynamical system), the larger root for the case with 𝛼 < 𝛽 is an unstable one. Therefore, for stable biogenesis for the generic values of the parameters, we assume 𝛼 > 𝛽 . 

> S4.2. Phase diagram for single cisterna

The roots of the above equation Eq. (S54) are given by the polynomial equation,                   

> 𝐶 1𝐶 2𝑎 0𝑣 +𝐶 1(𝑎 0𝑣 −𝑑 )𝑀 +𝐶 2(𝑣 +𝑎 0𝑣 )𝑀 2+ ( 𝑣 +𝑎 0𝑣 −𝑑 )𝑀 3=0(S57)

We use this polynomial equation to study the nature of intersections of the fission and fusion kernels, i.e., existence and stability of the fixed points for 𝑀 , by varying the effective parameters 𝑣 (influx rate), 𝐶 1, 𝐶 2 (levels of fisogens and fisogens), 𝑎 0 (nucleation constant) and 𝑑 (fission/exit rate). This allows us to construct a phase diagram. As shown in Fig. S9(a-c), there is a stable phase corresponding to one or two stable fixed points, unbounded phase corresponding to no stable fixed points, and semi-stable phase corresponding to one stable and one unstable fixed point, with asymptotic behaviour depending on the initial condition. Phases can be further classified based on the value of the steady state cisterna size - vesicle phase ( 𝑀 ≤ 𝑀 0, a threshold size and stable fixed point) and stable cisterna phase ( 𝑀 ≥ 𝑀 0 and stable fixed point), see Fig. S9(a-c). Phase boundaries can be constructed analytically by applying the transformation 𝑀 → 𝑀 +𝑀 0, to the cubic polynomial Eq.(S57), and then analysing the coefficients ( 𝐶 𝑓 𝑖 ’s) and the cubic discriminant ( Δ) of the transformed polynomial (Fig.S9(a-c)). To segregate the vesicle phase from the cisterna phase, we have taken 𝑀 0 = 1, the unit vesicle size, which gives the transformed polynomial,

Figure S9: Phase diagram and boundaries for single cisterna. (a,b,c) There are three phases – stable, semistable and unbounded. Stable phase includes vesicle and cisterna phases. At higher influx rate, cisterna size grows unbounded (blue region). A semistable phase, that correspond to to scenario when fusion and fission kernels intersect twice (Fig.S4(c)), and it is stable or unstable depending on the initial condition. The analytic phase boundaries can be computed from the cubic discriminant Δ and coefficients of cubic polynomial Eq.(S57). These phases are shown in 

(𝑣, 𝐶 1) and (𝑣, 𝐶 2) planes. (b) This phase diagram is similar to that of the microscopic model (see Fig.S5(b)) further justifying the map. Phases can be further classified based on value of cisterna size , into vesicle phase (M < 1, or a given threshold 𝑀 0 and stable fixed point, yellow region) and cisterna phase (M ≥ 1 and stable fixed point). As the influx rate is increased, stable cisterna size increases until fission and fusion kernels no more intersect each other (Fig.S4), and the there are no stable fixed points, causing cisterna size to grows unboundedly (light blue region). Default parameter values are, 𝐶 2 = 40, 𝐶 1 = 100, 𝑑 = 1, 𝑎 0 = 0.05. (c) For a given influx rate 𝑣 , a minimum value of the nucleation constant 𝑎 0 is needed for formation of a stable cisterna. (d) Fixes point structure of Eq.(S57) can also be represented as a catastrophe curve using the surface (Σ 𝑓 ) equation from Eq.(S59): Number of fixed points change as one moves in ( 𝑣, 𝑎 0)) plane (parameter values 𝐶 2 = 40, 𝐶 1 = 100, 𝑑 = 1), that can be enumerated from Table S2. Red plot points represent parameter regime with multistability whereas blue plot points represent parameter regime with single stable fixed point. (e) Dynamical systems such as, Eq.(S57), ensures neighborhood stability, i.e., the fixed point point structure of the system remains invariant under small changes of parameters (parameter values 𝐶 2 = 40, 𝐶 1 = 100, 

𝑑 = 1, 𝑎 0 = 0.05). If the above coefficient 𝐶 𝑓 0 < 0, the above polynomial cannot have a root in ≤ 1 (as the original polynomial Eq.(S57) will only cross the y-axis for 𝑀 > 𝑀 0). This is also depicted by the contour line given by 𝐶 𝑓 0 = 0 (thick purple line) in Fig.S9(a-c). Furthermore, Fig.S9(c)suggests that a minimum value of the nucleation constant 𝑎 0 is needed to move to the stable cisterna phase from the vesicle phase. A closer look at Eq.(S57) reveals that the system has cubic (cusp) singularity. In fact, equations such as Eq.(S57) correspond to a stable family of functions (unfolding) under small changes of the parameters, Fig.S9(e) [46]. Above equation Eq.(S57) loses (or gains) a pair of roots through saddle-node (SN) bifurcation[42] leading to change in the number of stable fixed points (one to two). A better way to represent such fixed-point structures is to look at catastrophe curves [46], which is the study of the appearance of singularities as one moves continuously in parameter space (Fig.S9(d)). The cubic polynomial Eq.(S57) above, can be mapped to canonical form of a cusp catastrophe curve (universal unfolding of 𝑥 4-singularity with codimesion 2, i.e. the control parameters, see [46, 47] for details). More elaborately, one needs to S5.1 Constructing dynamical system for two cisternae 38 compute the following surfaces [111],

where F𝑘 (𝑀, 𝜇 𝜇 𝜇 ) is a polynomial of degree-k with n-parameters 𝜇 𝜇 𝜇 , which in case of cusp catastrophe is 4 and 2 respectively, 

Σ 𝑓 is defined by the gradient and Δ 𝑓 by the Hessian determinant. Σ 𝑓 for the single cisterna dynamical system is plotted in Fig.S9(d). 

Appendix S5: Dynamics of the two cisternae system S5.1. Constructing dynamical system for two cisternae 

Here we derive the dynamical system for two cisternae. For this, we derive motivation from S3, where we have discussed Master equation formalism for two cisternae, and introduced intercisternal time over which intercisternal vesicle flux is integrated via Direct or Indirect scheme. The intercisternal flux depends on the availability of cognate pairs of v-SNAREs and t-SNAREs at the donor and the acceptor cisterna respectively [53–63]. Consider the fission flux 𝐽 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 𝑖,𝑖 +1 at time 𝑡 , from the donor cisterna 𝑖 to target cisterna 𝑖 + 1. The appropriate v-SNAREs need to be transported along with the cargo vesicles destined to the target cisterna, leading to a depletion of the SNARE-pool and a reduced probability for subsequent fission events at 𝑖 , unless replenished by fusion events at 𝑖 from 𝑖 + 1 [64]. This implies that the fission flux from the cisterna 𝑖 to 𝑖 + 1 at time 

𝑡 will depend on the local fisogen availability and the size 𝑀 𝑖 of the donor cisterna 𝑖 as well as on the local fusgogen availability and size 𝑀 𝑖 +1 of the acceptor cisterna 𝑖 + 1 at earlier times within a time window, functionally represented as the flux J𝑖,𝑖 +1 (𝜙 𝑥 𝑖 , 𝑀 𝑖 , 𝑡 ; 𝜙 𝑦 𝑖 +1, 𝑀 𝑖 +1, 𝑡 − 𝑡 𝑤 ). Here, 𝜙 variables keep track of SNARE identities 𝑥, 𝑦 , and hence give a cisternal SNARE-matching condition while 𝑡 𝑤 is intercisternal time window [27, 65, 66], which is drawn from a given probability distribution 𝑃 (𝑡 𝑤 ). Furthermore, the fusogen and fisogen availability may depend on the local cisterna size as well. Using same arguments, fission flux from cisterna 𝑖 + 1 to 𝑖 at time 𝑡 will depend on the local fisogen availability and the size of the donor cisterna 𝑖 as well as on the local fusgogen availability and size of the acceptor cisterna 𝑖 + 1 a earlier time within a time window functionally represented as the flux J𝑖 +1,𝑖 (𝜙 𝑦 𝑖 +1, 𝑀 𝑖 +1, 𝑡 ; 𝜙 𝑥 𝑖 , 𝑀 𝑖 , 𝑡 − 𝑡 𝑤 ). Summed over the SNARE identities and over the intercisternal times 𝑡 𝑤 , gives the total intercisternal flux between two cisterna, i.e. 

J 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 𝑖,𝑖 +1 (𝑀 𝑖 , 𝑀 𝑖 +1, 𝑡 ) =∑︁

We further assume 𝑃 (𝑡 𝑤 ) to be uniform over an interval (0, 𝑇 ) and a SNARE matching condition Í𝑥,𝑦 𝜙 𝑥 𝑖 (𝑡 )𝜙 𝑦 𝑖 +1 (𝑡 − 𝑡 𝑤 ) = 𝐶 𝑖,𝑖 +1 (𝑡, 𝑡 − 𝑡 𝑤 ) 𝛿 𝑥,𝑦 , 𝐶 𝑖,𝑖 +1 is a constant that encodes the coupling strength for different cargo identities and can depend on the intercisternal time window 𝑡 𝑤 . One can also have more intricate matching conditions, depending on whether one or both homotypic and heterotypic fusions are allowed [13]. where there are intercisternal fluxes, and we also include leak fluxes. 𝑣 𝑓 𝑢𝑠 𝑚 , 𝑣 ′ 𝑓 𝑖𝑠 𝑚 are characteristic cisterna size scales set by fusion and fission enzymes (see Eqs.S43,S45) and 1/ 𝜅 𝑖 𝑗 ’s are the time scales associated with fusion and fission kernels at the cisterna 𝑖 = 1, 2 ( 𝑗 = 1 for fusion and 𝑗 =2 for fission). 𝑇 1, 𝑇 2, 𝑇 3, 𝑇 4 are intercisternal times over which mass is transferred from from one cisterna to another (or glycosylation enzymes are delivered to the cisterna via retrograde transport [65, 66], which might act as a time marker). By scaling the integration time w.r.t. 𝜅 𝑖 𝑗 , one can see that if the intercisternal times are much smaller than fission and fusion times scales, 𝑇 1, 𝑇 2, , 𝑇 3, 𝑇 4 ≪ 1/𝜅 𝑖 𝑗 , the integral terms might vanish and there is no transfer of material from cisterna 1 to 2. Hence, to have a bounded as well as non-trivial contribution, the inverse time scale 𝑘 𝑖 𝑗 and the window 𝑇 1, 𝑇 2, 𝑇 3, 𝑇 4 must be properly balanced so that their product 

𝜆 𝛼 = 𝑘 𝑖 𝑗 𝑇 𝛼 remains finite. This is physically plausible, as 𝑇 𝛼 can be seen to encode unaccounted degrees of freedom (such as spatial variables) while 𝑘 𝑖 𝑗 increases as these degrees of freedom are condensed (i.e., 𝑇 𝛼 → 0). Furthermore, the condition 𝑇 𝛼 ≪ 1/𝜆 𝛼 ensures that the integration window is small relative to the characteristic evolution time of the system. Under these conditions, the memory effects become negligible (can also be seen from taylor expansion of the integral), and the integro-differential dynamics reduce to a standard ODE.

> S5.3. Nullclines and bifurcations

The changes in the solution structure of one cisterna dynamical systems can be completely captured by saddle-node bifurcation . However, as the dimensionality of the system increases, other possibilities also arise. For instance, the stability of a fixed point might change via Hopf bifurcation , and the system can admit closed orbit solutions [42]. Unlike the one-cisterna case, finding the root structure of high-dimensional systems requires quite advanced mathe-matical tools (see Intersection theory [72], for instance), and we will rely on graphical and numerical techniques. In this regard, we plot the nullclines , which are the curves of zero dynamics of different state variables in the state space. The intersections of these curves yield the system’s fixed points, as all the rates of change are simultaneously zero at those locations. We draw the nullclines for the system Eqs.(S68),(S69) (or Eqs.(12),(13) in the main text) in Fig.S11 and analyse how it changes as we vary the system parameters. Let us discuss the detailed root structure (and the phases) for the two cisternae dynamical system. Suppose we fix the values of the parameters at, 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100 , 𝐶 12 = 𝐶 22 = 20 , 𝑎 1 = 𝑎 2 = 0.05 , 𝑑 2 = 1, 𝑑 12 = 1.2, 𝑑 21 = 0 and draw the flows and nullclines as we increase the influx rate 𝑣 , Fig.S11 (a-f). With these parameter values and small 𝑣 , there is only one stable fixed point near zero, i.e., there is no cisterna formation. As the influx rate increases, a pair of fixed points appear via saddle node bifurcation , and the number of fixed points goes from 1 → 3 → 5 in Fig.S11 (a-c). As 𝑣 increases further, the second cisterna starts to grow in size and the fixed points disappear via another saddle node bifurcation .At this point, locally unstable limit cycle solution appear via Hopf bifurcation (Fig.S11 (d)). Thereafter, increments in S5.3 Nullclines and bifurcations 42

Figure S11: Solution structure for two cisternae . Solution structure for two cisternae dynamical system is characterized by fixed point stability – stable fixed point(S), saddle fixed point (D) and unstable fixed point(U) as well as via bifurcations – saddle-node bifurcations (SN 𝑖 ’s), Hopf bifurcation (H), SNIC bifurcation and SNLC bifurcation. As influx rate is increased from (a) to (c), the system goes through a series of saddle-node bifurcations, leading to change in the number of fixed points. (a-inset) At very high influx rate ( 𝑣 = 2), system has no fixed points and it grows unboundedly. (d) A fixed point can change its stability via Hopf bifurcation, and an unstable local limit cycle arises. (e) As 𝑣 increases, a saddle-node pair near zero annihilate each other and since they lie on a close loop, it gives rise to an outer limit cycle solution via SNIC (light blue, dashed). Further increasing 𝑣 , the inner unstable limit cycle (brown, dashed) grows in size and annihilates with the outer stable limit cycle via SNLC and the system is left with a stable fixed point. (f) Bifurcation diagram depicting the series of events (a-e). Other parameters are kept fixed at, 

𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100, 𝐶 12 = 𝐶 22 = 20, 𝑎 1 = 𝑎 2 = 0.05 , 𝑑 2 = 1, 𝑑 12 = 1.2, 𝑑 21 = 0. (g) The enclosed fixed point is unstable for parameter values, 𝑑 12 = 3, 𝑑 2 = 2.4 and the generated limit cycle via SNIC bifurcation is globally stable, also seen in the bifurcation diagram (h). The corresponding phases are shown in the phase diagram Fig.S13(a). (i,j) A globally stable limit cycle via SNIC can also be obtained by lowering the value of exit rate, 𝑑 2 = 0.8. (k,l) Adding a 𝑀 1-independent retrograde flux from cisterna-2 to 1 (𝑑 21 = 0.6) modifies the nullclines and a locally stable limit cycle appears via SNIC. 

𝑣 , leads to disappearance of fixed points via saddle node bifurcation near zero, and if the saddle-node pair happens to S5.3 Nullclines and bifurcations 43 lie on a closed loop (facilitated by the positivity and nonlinearity of flux kernels), their disappearance leads to the rise of limit cycles via SNIC bifurcation in Fig.S11(e) (light blue,dashed) and we have a nested bistable limit cycle solution with stable central fixed point. Furthermore, the inner unstable circle Fig.S11(e) (brown,dashed) keeps on growing in size, eventually coalescing and annihilating with the outer stable limit cycle via saddle-node bifurcation of limit cycles (SNLC) and the system is left with a stable fixed point. The series of events (a-e) is depicted in a comprehensive way in the bifurcation diagram, Fig.S11(f). Note that for the system Eqs.(S68),(S69), the stability of the limit cycle solution generated via SNIC bifurcation depends upon the fixed point structure remaining after the saddle-node annihilation. Crucially, if only one enclosed fixed point remains after the bifurcation, the stability of the limit cycle generated via SNIC can be determined by the stability of the enclosed fixed point and the global flows in the system. The stability of the obtained limit cycle solutions changes if other parameters in the system are varied. For example, if we set 𝑑 12 = 3, 𝑑 2 = 2.4 (Fig.S11(g,h), then the SNIC bifurcation precedes the formation of an unstable limit cycle via Hopf and the obtained limit cycle is globally stable, as shown in the bifurcation diagram, Fig.S11(h). A globally stable limit cycle via SNIC is also achieved by lowering the value of exit rate 𝑑 2, Fig.S11(i,j). Futhermore, adding a 𝑀 1-independent retrograde flux from cisterna 2 to 1 can lead to the formation of locally stable limit cycles via SNIC bifurcation, Fig.S11(k,l). Based on the insight above, in the main text, we have explored the following solution classes and the bifurcations leading to the transitions between them: (a) Appearance or annihilation of a pair of fixed points via saddle node bifurcation. (b) Change of stability of fixed point via Hopf bifurcation: This might lead to appearance of local oscillations. (c) Appearance of limit cycle via SNIC bifurcation: This might lead to the appearance of locally stable, bistable or globally stable oscillations. SNIC bifurcations are identified by a saddle-node (SN) ghost [42] near the point of saddle-node annihilation, where the system spends a significant amount of time compared to the rest of the trajectory, contributing significantly to the time period of the oscillation. (d) Coalescence via SNLC: The Saddle-Node Bifurcation of Limit Cycles (SNLC) involves the collision and mutual annihilation of two nested limit cycles. Typically, an inner unstable cycle expands to merge with an enclosing stable cycle; upon coalescence, both periodic solutions vanish. Analogous to the saddle-node bifurcation of fixed points, this transition is accompanied by a ghost delay where trajectories linger in the vicinity of the ”phantom” orbits before eventually drifting away. (e) Note that when the system has multistability, i.e., the solutions are only locally stable, the asymptotic behaviour of the system depends on the initial conditions (Fig.S13(d,e,f)), resulting in overlapping regions in the phase diagram over the parameter space of the system.  

> S5.4. Effects of retrograde flux on the solution space of the two-cisternae system

To understand the effects of the retrograde flux on the solution space of 2-cisternae system, let us look at the system of equation, Eqs. (S68),(S69). It can be written as,

(S79) Note from Eqs. (S68),(S69), the retrograde flux 𝐻 2 (𝑀 1, 𝑀 2) is an increasing function of the sizes of the first and the second cisterna, 𝑀 1 and 𝑀 2, respectively. We can see that adding retrograde flux might cause the off-diagonal elements of the Jacobian, Eq. (S78) to have the same sign and hence, limits the possibility of limit cycle solutions, see Fig. S13(c). We have seen before that adding a 𝑀 1-independent retrograde flux from cisterna 2 to 1 can lead to the formation of a locally stable limit cycles via SNIC bifurcation (Fig. S11). Adding a retrograde flux that is dependent on both 𝑀 1, 𝑀 2,adds to the nonlinearities in the system and in general, would lead to increase in the number of fixed points. We discuss a consequence of this below. In the main text, we have discussed the response of the 2-cisternae system to systematic perturbation that involves dissolution of the second cisterna by increasing the exit rate, 𝑑 2 at the second cisterna. We observed that the 2-cisternae system has different response based on the ratrograde rate. We explain these solution using the nullclines and the flows (Fig. S12) for these two parameter regimes. Figure S12: Effect of retrograde flux on the response to systematic perturbations of the 2-cisternae system. (a,b) For the 2-cisternae system with low retrograde rate 𝑑 21 (parameter values shown in the plots) compared to the anterograde rate 𝑑 12 , if the exit rate at the second cisterna, 𝑑 2 is increased (2 .2 → 4.2) in (a) → (b), the second cisterna is depleted. As the anterograde flux from the first cisterna increases with the size of the second cisterna (see Eqs. (S68),(S69)), depletion of the second cisterna causes the influx rate dominating the outflux rate at the first cisterna, leading to the increase in the size of the first cisterna and eventual unbounded growth. The solutions are shown in (c). (d,e) If the retrograde rate 𝑑 21 (parameter values shown in the plots) is comparable to the anterograde rate 𝑑 12 , increasing the exit rate, 𝑑 2 leads to depletion of the second cisterna, as before. However, in this regime, even when the second cisterna is depleted by increasing 𝑑 2 (0 .55 → 0.65 in (d) → (e)), outflux flux ( = anterograde flux + leak flux) at the first cisterna can balance the influx rate, 𝑣 supporting a stable vesicle phase (see Eqs. (S68),(S69)). The solutions are shown in (f). Other parameters are kept fixed at 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100, 𝐶 22 = 20, 𝐶 12 = 20, 𝑑 1 = 1. (i) At first, let us consider the case with small retrograte rate. In this case, if the exit rate 𝑑 2 is large (keeping the influx rate, 𝑣 at a constant value), the second cisterna is depleted. However, as a result of this, not only 𝑑 21 decreases, but the anterograde rate 𝑑 12 decreases as well, see Eqs. (S68),(S69). This leads to the first cisterna becoming unstable, see Fig. S12(a-c). (ii) Now, consider the case with large retrograte rate. In this case, if the exit rate 𝑑 2 is large (keeping the influx rate, 

𝑣 at a constant value), the second cisterna is depleted, same as case (i). However, large retrograde flux implies that stable phases for this system occur at smaller values of the influx rate, 𝑣 . In this regime, if the retrograde flux is reduced by depleting the second cisterna, the anterograde flux and the leak flux at the first cisterna (see Eqs. (S68),(S69)) are large enough to support a stable vesicle phase. This effectively leads to depletion of the first cisterna, see Fig. S12(d-f). 

S5.5. Arriving at the phase diagram and phase boundaries for the two-cisternae system 

As mentioned above, in this work we are concerned with local bifurcations (saddle-node, Hopf) as well as global bifurcations (SNIC, SNLC). In this section, we present the method to predict local as well as global bifurcations based on eigenvalue analysis at the fixed points of a dynamical system. Although the eigenvalue analysis cannot completely predict a global bifurcation, we will see that if we study the solution structure of the system over the parameter space of the system, the change in the stability of the fixed points in the system, as we vary the parameters, may hint towards the kind of global bifurcations that might have caused the change in the solution class of the system. Let us first discuss the eigenvalue analysis involving saddle-node and Hopf bifurcations. For the 2-cisternae dynamical S5.5 Arriving at the phase diagram and phase boundaries for the two-cisternae system 45

> (c)
> (d) (e) (f )

Figure S13: Phase diagram for two cisternae. (a) Phase diagram for two cisternae system in (𝑣, 𝑑 2) plane. The horizontal line corresponds to the bifurcation diagram in Fig.S11(h). Phase boundaries, or bifurcation lines, are constructed by identifying parameter values where the real part of the Jacobian eigenvalue crosses zero (gray dashed lines, indicating a saddle-node, SN bifurcation), and where a pair of complex conjugate eigenvalues crosses the imaginary axis (blue dashed lines, indicating a Hopf bifurcation) [42, 43]. (b) Phase diagram for two cisternae system in (𝑣, 𝑑 12 ) plane. (c) Phase diagram for two cisternae system in (𝑣, 𝑑 21 ) plane. For the flux kernels used in Eqs.(S68),(S69) (and equivalently, Eqs.(12),(13)), oscillatory solutions are observed for small retrograde rate, 𝑑 21 . For (a,b,c), default values of the parameters are: 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100, 𝐶 12 = 𝐶 22 = 20, 𝑎 1 = 𝑎 2 = 0.05 , 𝑑 2 = 1, 𝑑 12 = 3, 𝑑 21 = 0. (d,e,f) There are also regions with multistability, with mixed phases where system converges to one phase or the other depending on the initial condition. For instance, (d) semistable single cisterna phase, with 𝑀 1 > 1, 𝑀 2 < 1 (e) unstable two cisterna phase with stable fixed point lying at 𝑀 1 > 1, 𝑀 2 < 1. (f) System may grow unboundedly (blue) or goes to a stable fixed point (orange) depending on the initial condition. (S80) resulting in the characteristic equation 𝜆 2 − Tr (𝐽 )𝜆 + Det (𝐽 ) = 0, where Tr (𝐽 ), Det (𝐽 ) are trace and determinant of the Jacobian matrix 𝐽 , respectively. To have stable solutions (eigenvalues with negative real parts), we must have Tr (𝐽 ) < 0and Det (𝐽 ) > 0. The conditions for the saddle-node bifurcation are – det (𝐽 ) = 0, Tr (𝐽 ) ≠ 0. The steady state loses stability when the trace changes sign from negative to positive (with Det (𝐽 ) > 0), and periodic solutions typically arise in this region via Hopf bifurcation ([113]). These changes in the number and stability of fixed points, when plotted in the parameter space, give the bifurcation boundaries. In 2D, these conditions are fairly simple [114] and can be tabulated in Table S3. Note that these conditions are related to the 2-dimensional version of the Routh–Hurwitz criterion [115], which essentially gives conditions on the coefficients of the characteristic polynomial for the emergence of a pair of imaginary roots in higher-order systems [74] and therefore provides a tool to study the stability boundaries and the rise of limit cycles. As mentioned above, predicting a SNIC bifurcation, which is a global bifurcation, strictly from local analysis is impossible. However, as we can see from the nullcline structure and bifurcation diagrams in Fig.S11, for the two dimensional system considered here, the stability of the resultant limit cycle is topologically linked to the enclosed fixed point. A stable limit cycle arises via SNIC if it encloses an unstable fixed point, Fig.S11(g,h), while a central stable fixed point can give rise to a concentric bistable system with a stable fixed point surrounded by an unstable limit cycle and a stable limit cycle, Fig.S11(e,f). Therefore, monitoring the local instability of the central steady state provides a necessary (though not sufficient, sufficiency provided by non-linear flow) precursor condition for the emergence of stable oscillations via this global mechanism. Fig.S13(a,b,c), depicts the boundaries of saddle-node (grey, dashed) and Hopf bifurcation (blue, dashed) in the param-eter space. However, this analysis does not predict the basin of a fixed point or limit cycle, and the complete numerical phase diagram has to be computed numerically. We use the following time domain numerical scheme for finding these phases: 1. Solve the differential equations Eqs.(S68),(S69) in (𝑀 1, 𝑀 2) (Eqs.(12),(13) in the main text) for given values of parameters and initial conditions. 2. Choose, say, five time points 𝜏 𝑎 < 𝜏 𝑏 < 𝜏 𝑐 < 𝜏 𝑑 < 𝜏 𝑒 , these time points are taken to be large enough numbers to make sure that the system has arrived at a steady-state. We compute the following quantities : |𝑀 1 (𝜏 𝑎 ) − 

𝑀 1 (𝜏 𝑐 )| , |𝑀 1 (𝜏 𝑏 ) − 𝑀 1 (𝜏 𝑑 )| , |𝑀 2 (𝜏 𝑎 ) − 𝑀 2 (𝜏 𝑐 )| , |𝑀 2 (𝜏 𝑐 ) − 𝑀 2 (𝜏 𝑑 )| , 𝑀 1 (𝑡 = 𝜏 𝑒 ), 𝑀 2 (𝑡 = 𝜏 𝑒 ).3. The steady-state is a fixed point solution if 0 < |𝑀 1 (𝜏 𝑎 ) − 𝑀 1 (𝜏 𝑐 )| < 𝛿 1, 0 < |𝑀 1 (𝜏 𝑏 ) − 𝑀 1 (𝜏 𝑑 )| < 𝛿 1, 0 < |𝑀 2 (𝜏 𝑎 ) −

While the numerical scheme presented above is adequate for the present purposes, it is subject to the following limitations – (i) it would not detect limit cycle solutions with a radius 𝑟 𝜏 , such that 𝑟 𝜏 < 𝛿 1 or 𝑟 𝜏 > 𝑀 𝑡 ℎ – and would assume them to be fixed point solutions. (ii) Fixed point solutions that reach the asymptotic value at a time much larger than 𝜏 𝑒 , will be categorized as unbounded solutions. 

Computing the phase difference: 

Since the solutions of the above dynamical system have nonlinear waveform, and frequency of the system is amplitude dependent, usual methods such as Fourier or Hilbert transform might give inaccurate results (Bedrosian theorem might not hold [116]) and computing the phase difference between the two time series is not straightforward. If the system has well-defined peaks, we can use peak to peak analysis to compute the phase difference using the following procedure – For each signal, detect consecutive peaks and assign phase linearly between them (0, 2𝜋 ). The phase difference between two signals/time-series is simply: phase difference, Δ𝜙 (𝑡 ) = 2𝜋 (time between peaks/time period). We use a variant of this method – we compute the time period 𝜏 from the numerical solution and fit a sinusoidal function 𝐴 1 + 𝐴 2 𝑠𝑖𝑛 (2𝜋𝑡 /𝜏 + 𝑐 )

to the solutions obtained for 𝑀 1 and 𝑀 2 from the differential equations Eqs.(12),(13). The phase difference can then be derived from the correlation of the fitted functions. Note that the sinusoidal fitting extracts phase from the entire waveform’s distribution and is a better representative of the true temporal relationship between broad or asymmetric signals than peak to peak’s single-point estimate. It essentially captures the “mean behaviour” or the macroscopic phase shift of the oscillators. 

> Appendix S6: Numerical computation of structural stability of the 2-cisternae dynamical system

In this section, we prove the structural stability for the case of two cisternae. To compute structural stability, we follow the numerical scheme given below. For clarity, we explain these steps when applied to cubic polynomial/cusp singularity (which is our single cisterna system). Readers interested in further details can consult [46, 47, 86]. 1. Compute the Gr¨ obner basis from the system of equations/vector fields (e.g. 𝑥 3 + 𝑏 𝑥 + 𝑐 for cusp singularity [47], where x is the state variable and b and c are constant parameters). We have used Mathematica for this (which uses Buchberger algorithm and DegreeReverseLexicographic order, see [86] for details). 2. Extract the leading monomials from each polynomial in the Gr¨ obner basis (e.g., {𝑥 3 }). 3. Generate all monomials up to a chosen maximum polynomial Degree: {1, 𝑥, 𝑥 2, 𝑥 3, 𝑥 4, 𝑥 5, . . . }.4. Reject all monomials divisible by any leading monomial (e.g., reject 𝑥 3, 𝑥 4, 𝑥 5, . . . ). 5. The surviving monomials form the standard monomial basis (e.g., {1, 𝑥, 𝑥 2 }). 6. Algebraic capacity = dimension of standard monomial basis (e.g., capacity = 3). 7. Choose specific numerical values for the parameters and numerically solve the system to count the number of distinct complex roots (geometric roots). 8. Compare: if geometric roots = algebraic capacity, the system is structurally stable. 48 9. If geometric roots < algebraic capacity, the system is structurally unstable. The above steps for the 2-cisternae system are tabulated in Table S4. The number of distinct complex roots and the algebraic capacity are plotted in Fig. S14.

Table S4: Establishing structural stability of two-cisternae dynamical system. Given a dynamical system, its fixed points are determined by the components of its vector field. If these fixed points can be determined by polynomial equations, one can compute a Gr¨ obner basis [86] for the system to extract the leading monomials and construct the standard monomial basis. The cardinality of this standard basis yields the algebraic capacity of the system. The fixed points of the dynamical system Eqs.(S68),(S69) (Eqs.(12),(13) in the main text) can be computed numerically, parameter values 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100 , 𝐶 12 = 𝐶 22 = 20 , 𝑣 = 1, 𝑑 12 = 3, 𝑎 1 = 𝑎 2 = 0.05 , 𝑑 1 = 𝑑 2 = 1, 𝑑 21 = 1. The system’s fixed points are considered structurally stable if the number of distinct complex roots it admits exactly matches this algebraic capacity, ensuring that all roots are simple and non-degenerate. This implies that system is locally structurally stable [47, 73] which ensures neighborhood stability, i.e., the fixed point point structure of the system remains invariant under small changes of parameters. The number of distinct roots and the algebraic capacity are plotted in Fig. S14. 

Appendix S7: Robustness of phases to extrinsic and intrinsic noise S7.1. Robustness to extrinsic noise 

As suggested in the above section and III, the dynamical system Eq.(6) corresponds to a family of functions with the root structure, robust under small changes in parameters. The structural stability of the system further ensures that the root structure of the dynamical system is robust to intrinsic and extrinsic noise. In the following calculations, we subject the dynamical system corresponding to single and multiple cisternae to extrinsic and intrinsic noise and look for bounds on the noise strength for the solutions to remain in the same solution class. To quantify the robustness to extrinsic noise, we assume that each cisterna 𝑖 with size 𝑀 𝑖 is driven by an extrinsic multiplicative noise with noise strength 𝜉 𝑖 (𝑀 𝑖 ),where the multiplicative noise strength at the cisterna 𝑖 is a function of size of the cisterna 𝑖 . This implicitly assumes that extrinsic fluctuations in the size of a cisterna is only due to local factors, such as local availability of vesicles and fisogens-fusogens or local hydrodynamic interactions [117–119]. With this, the dynamical system for a system of cisternae driven by extrinsic multiplicative noise is given by,

> 𝜉 (𝑠 )𝑑𝑠
> 
> =⇒𝑑 log (𝑃 ∗(𝑀 ))
> 𝑑𝑀 =
> 
> 𝑓 (𝑠 ) − 12𝜉 ′(𝑠 )
> 
> 𝜉 (𝑠 )(S85)

where N is the normalization factor. Hence, whereas the fixed points of the deterministic system are given by roots of 

𝑓 (𝑀 ), the extrema of the distribution 𝑃 (𝑀 ) are given by roots of   𝑓 (𝑀 ) − 12 𝜉 ′ (𝑀 ), defined as shape function in [87]. Hence, the extrema of the probability distribution corresponding to the stochastic system Eq.(S83) is given by, 

(− 1 + 𝑣 + 𝑎 0 𝑣 − 𝜖 )𝑀 3 + ( 𝑣 + 𝑎 0 𝑣 − 𝜖 )𝐶 2 𝑀 2 + 𝐶 1 (− 1 + 𝑎 0 𝑣 − 𝜖 )𝑀 + 𝐶 1 𝐶 2 (𝑎 0 𝑣 − 𝜖 ) = 0 (S86) It is clear that adding multiplicative noise of this functional form effectively leads to a modified version of Eq.(7) with nucleation rate 𝑣 𝑎 0 → ( 𝑣 𝑎 0 − 𝜖 ) and for 𝜖 > 𝑎 0 𝑣 , sign signature of the sequence of coefficients for the above polynomial changes, leading to the change in number of fixed points. For instance, consider the bistable regime for the deterministic system corresponding to Eq.(S83). In this regime the sign signature for the sequence of coefficients is ( −, +, −, +), see Table S2. As the noise strength is increased, 𝜖 > 𝑎 0𝑣 , the sign signature changes to ( −, +, −, −) and the fixed points near zero are destroyed (Fig. S15(a)) and the system has just one positive root left. On further increasing the noise strength, 

𝜖 > (𝑎 0 + 1)𝑣 , sign signature changes to ( −, −, −, −) , the fixed point away from zero is destroyed as well and the system has no fixed points. The same can be seen from the Fokker-Planck equation. As we see in Fig. S15(b), steady state distribution of Fokker-Planck equation for one cisterna is bimodal for low noise ( 𝜖 << 𝑎 0 𝑣 ), it becomes unimodal for high noise ( 𝜖 ≈ 𝑎 0 𝑣 ), and the system is without any maxima for 𝜖 > (𝑎 0 + 1)𝑣 .The extension of Eq.(S85) to two cisterna, Eqs.(S68),(S69) (Eqs. (12),(13) in the main text) is not always possible as it requires integrability (potential) conditions [88, 120, 121], not satisfied by the dynamical system Eqs.(S68),(S69). With the potential condition (zero nonequilibrium current [120]), robustness bounds for the root structure of the two cisternae case are similar to robustness bounds for the one cisterna case. As before, if we assume that extrinsic fluctuations in the size of a cisterna is only due to local factors, addition of cisternal size dependent extrinsic multiplicative noise for two cisternae case gives,             

> ¤𝑀 1=𝜇 1(𝑀 1, 𝑀 2) + √︁ 𝜉 1(𝑀 1)𝜂 1¤𝑀 2=𝜇 2(𝑀 1, 𝑀 2) + √︁ 𝜉 2(𝑀 2)𝜂 2(S87)

where 𝜇 1, 𝜇 2 are given by deterministic dynamics in (S68), (S69) and 𝜂 1, 𝜂 2 are Gaussian white noises. Furthermore, we analyse the change in root structure for 𝜉 1 (𝑀 1) = 𝜖 1 𝑀 1, 𝜉 2 (𝑀 2) = 𝜖 2 𝑀 2, i.e., stochastic vesicle addition and removal is proportional to the cisterna size, as for single cisterna case. Following steps similar to Eq.(S85), it gives that the system remains in vesicle phase for small noise ( 𝜖 1/2+𝜖 2/2 < 𝑣 𝑎 1 in Eqs.(S87),(S68), (S69)) but goes to the cisternae phase as the noise strength is increased, as the roots near zero are destroyed. This can be seen in the stochastic trajectory, Fig.S15(c) as well as with the steady state distribution of the corresponding Fokker-Planck system, Fig.S15(d). Furthermore, for two cisternae, the system can also go from the cisternae phase (VT) to the limit cycle (CP) phase in the presence of noise (Fig.S15(e,f)). This happens as the noise strength of the kind 𝜖 𝑖 𝑀 𝑖 modifies the nucleation rate, as mentioned above and therefore changes the effective bifurcation parameters for the system to enter the oscillatory regime, see the the phase diagram, Fig. S15(g) in (𝑣, 𝑎 0 𝑣 ) plane. The above analysis can in principle be extended to a general class of noises which are smooth positive functions of cisternal size, but this is beyond the scope of this work. Furthermore, in the bistable parameter regime, system can be in one of the states with escape rate given by Kramers’ barrier [120]. This can be used in devising a safety mechanism to have robust control mechanism to mitigate the spurt of material flux from ER, which is a subject of future work. Figure S15: Robustness of stable cisternal phases under extrinsic noise . (a) We study the robustness of the solutions of single cisterna system in the bistable regime, under extrinsic noise with strength 𝜖 𝑀 . The vesicle phase of the bistable 1-cisterna system (fixed point near zero, blue) is robust up to noise strength 𝜖 ∼ 𝑣 𝑎 0, and the system settles at the larger fixed point (red, cisterna phase) as 𝜖 is increased whereas the cisterna phase (fixed point away from zero) is robust up to noise strength 𝜖 ∼ 𝑣 (𝑎 0 + 1), beyond which the system is unregulated (green). Parameter values are, 𝑣 = 0.5, 𝐶 1 = 100, 𝐶 2 = 40, 𝑎 0 = 0.05. (b) This can also be seen from steady state probability distribution of Fokker-Planck equation which goes from bimodal (blue) to unimodal (red) as noise strength increases. (c,d) For 2-cisternae system, from proposition 1, the system only admits real eigenvalues if 𝐽 12 is a function of 𝑀 1 and 𝐽 21 is function of 𝑀 2 alone, i.e., 𝐽 12 = 𝑑 12 𝑀 1/( 𝐶 12 + 𝑀 1) and 𝐽 21 = 𝑑 21 𝑀 2/( 𝐶 22 + 𝑀 2) (see Eqs. (12), (13)) and has only fixed point solutions. For a multistable 2-cisternae real system, ( 𝑀 1, 𝑀 2) can go from vesicle phase to cisterna phase as noise strength is increased, seen from the stochastic differential system (c) and corresponding Fokker-Planck system (d). (d) (inset) depicts the vesicle solution for low noise. Parameter values, 𝑣 = 1.45, 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100, 𝐶 22 = 20, 𝐶 12 = 20, 

𝑑 12 = 1, 𝑑 21 = 0.3, 𝑑 1 = 1, 𝑑 2 = 1.5, 𝑎 1 = 𝑎 2 = 0.05. (e) If 𝐽 12 , 𝐽 21 are functions of both 𝑀 1 and 𝑀 2, system can have oscillatory solutions. Furthermore, system can also go from the vesicle phase to the limit cycle phase in the presence of noise ( 𝜖 𝑖 𝑀 𝑖 , 𝑖 = 1, 2). (f) This can also be seen from the solution of corresponding Fokker-Planck system. (inset) depicts the vesicle solution for low noise and as noise strength increases, the steady state probability distribution acquires two maxima corresponding to oscillatory solution. Hence, multiplicative noise may cause noisy system to mimic oscillations by changing the effective size dependence of the fluxes, and hence the class of solution it would admit. Parameter values, 𝑣 = 1, 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100, 𝐶 22 = 20, 𝐶 12 = 20, 𝑑 12 = 1.67, 𝑑 21 = 0.5, 𝑑 2 = 0.83, 𝑎 1 = 𝑎 2 = 0.05. (g) Phase diagram in (𝑎 0 𝑣, 𝑣 ) plane with 𝑀 1-independent retrograde flux. parameter values are, 𝐶 11 = 𝐶 21 = 100, 𝐶 22 = 20, 𝐶 12 = 20, 

𝑑 12 = 2.5, 𝑑 21 = 0.85, 𝑑 2 = 0.4, 𝑎 1 = 𝑎 2 = 0.05. Crucially, if the noise strength is of the functional form √𝜖 𝑖 𝑀 𝑖 , it effectively tweaks the the nucleation rate causing the asymptotic system behaviour to shift in the phase diagram and leading to change in the solution class. Various phases for this system is vesicle phase, stable single cistena phase (1 cist), stable 2-cisternae phase (2 cist) and limit cycle phase (LC).

Gillespie algorithm is used to generate the stochastic trajectories with the above propensities from which the asymptotic mean size and variation about the mean for one or multiple cisternae system can be computed for a given range of parameters. Just like the deterministic case, the mean value can be used to generate a phase diagram that accounts for intrinsic noise. For single cisterna, the modified phase diagrams (Fig.S16(a)) are similar to those of the deterministic case (Fig. S9(b)), but differs in some regions, especially near the vesicle-cisterna phase boundaries. However, when one computes the coefficient of variation (CV, i.e., the ratio of the standard deviation to the mean), one observes these anomalous regions to have, CV > 1 (Fig.S16(b)), suggesting the behaviour is dominated by copy number fluctuations in these regions and is not captured by mean value analysis. Similar trend is observed for the modified phase diagrams for two cisterna case (Fig.S16(c,d)), compared to phase diagram Fig.S13(a). 

Appendix S8: De novo cisternal assembly time S8.1. De novo assembly of single cisterna 

In the main text, we have used the functional form of the de novo formation time for single cisterna (III). In this section, we sketch a non-perturbative method for estimating this, which involves computation of time taken by a general nonlinear system to escape a saddle-node ghost and reach the asymptotic solution.
